b'By Reuters'
b"AUGUSTA Ga. (Reuters) - Rory McIlroy's desire to complete his collection of golf's four majors with a win at this week's Masters has led the Northern Irishman to try a fresh approach, one that focuses on meditation and juggling."
b"McIlroy enters the year's first major as the hottest player on the planet and he credits his solid start to 2019 with a new attitude that is all about perspective, persistence and poise, or what he refers to as the three P's."
b"The new formula for McIlroy, who will play the opening two rounds this week with American Rickie Fowler and Australia's Cameron Smith, centres around meditation, juggling and mind training."
b'"Look, I\'m not going to go and live with the monks for a couple months in Nepal, but just to be able to get your mind in the right place and be able to focus and to centre yourself," McIlroy told a news conference at Augusta National on Tuesday.'
b'"It\'s 10 minutes a day. It\'s not as if I\'m being consumed by it. But definitely something that has helped from time to time. Especially in situations where you need your mind to be right."'
b'McIlroy has five consecutive top-10 finishes at the Masters yet his defining moment at Augusta National remains his final-round collapse in 2011 when he failed to close out the win despite entering the day with a four-shot lead.'
b"But McIlroy, who has since gone on to win four times at golf's three other majors, has visions of becoming just the sixth person to complete the career grand slam and hopes his new approach will prove fruitful."
b'The 29-year-old Northern Irishman has a top-six finish in each of the six stroke play events he has played this year, including a triumph at the Players Championship where he meditated for 20 minutes ahead of the final round.'
b'Early indications, therefore, are that McIlroy may be on the right path with his meditation and juggling.'
b'"They mightn\'t be the right things for other people, but I think I\'ve found a formula that works for me, and I\'m\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x91\xe2\x80\x91 you know, I\'m going to persist with it and I\'m going to stick to it," said McIlroy.'
b'"It\'s helped me play some of the best golf of my career so far this year, and you know, hopefully that will continue."'
b'When it comes to juggling, McIlroy described himself as a "rookie" who can only handle three balls at once.'
b'"You know, maybe what resonates with me is isn\'t going to resonate with someone else, but I found what I feel is the best path forward for me and I\'ve committed to it," McIlroy said.'
b'McIlroy, who had another close call last year when he began the final round three shots behind eventual winner Patrick Reed, said the pressure he puts on himself at Augusta National compared to his previous appearances has changed.'
b'"I would dearly love to win this tournament one day. If it doesn\'t happen this week, that\'s totally fine, I\'ll come back next year and have another crack at it," said McIlroy.'
b'"But I\'m happy with where everything is, body, mind, game."'
b' (Reporting by Frank Pingue; Editing by Toby Davis)'

b'The strong finish was not enough as Reed managed to hang on for a one-stroke victory but Fowler, after getting over the pain of another near miss, was able to take plenty of positives from his performance.'
b'"The way I executed on the back nine Sunday last year was definitely something I pull from, and it was a lot of fun to be in the mix, birdieing 18, to make Patrick earn it a bit," said Fowler.'
b'"But I was just a little bit too far back, and Patrick put together a strong week of golf.'
b'"So it was fun. It was great to be in the mix, and like I said, have a chance. But time to do one better."'

b'SPRAGUE, Conn. \xe2\x80\x94 A bobcat that attacked a golfer in Connecticut was beaten off by golf clubs before environmental police tracked it down and killed it, authorities said.'
b'The bobcat attacked the golfer at about 8:30 a.m. Thursday on the Mohegan Sun Golf Course in the Baltic section of Sprague, according to a statement from the state Department of Energy and Environmental Protection.'
b"General manager Philip Krick Jr. told The Day of New London a foursome was near a fairway bunker on the seventh hole when the cat jumped on one man's back. Krick and police said the man in his 60s and another golfer in his group used their clubs to beat the cat away."
b'Environmental police tracked the cat to a culvert and shot it. The bobcat is thought to be the same animal that attacked a horse stabled nearby earlier in the morning. The horse received lacerations to the neck and eye and was treated by a veterinarian.'
b'The bobcat is being tested for rabies.'
b'The golfer was taken to a Norwich hospital to be treated for scratches. His name was not made public.'
b'Bobcats are common in Connecticut, but they tend to be secretive animals and attacks on humans are rare, state officials said. The last report of a bobcat attack in the state was in Bozrah in August 2014. That animal turned out to be rabid.'

b'On Golf'
b'By Karen Crouse'
b'RANCHO MIRAGE, Calif. \xe2\x80\x94 Albane Valenzuela could have been teeing it up Wednesday in the inaugural Augusta National Women\xe2\x80\x99s Amateur, where the contestants who survive the cut will play the final 18 holes on the fabled course where men will compete at the Masters next week.'
b'Valenzuela, a junior at Stanford and the fifth-ranked women\xe2\x80\x99s amateur in the world, seriously entertained the invitation from the board of governors of the Augusta National Golf Club until she received what she considered a better offer for this week: her third start in the ANA Inspiration, the women\xe2\x80\x99s first major of the year.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cGetting to tee off and play the same course as the world No. 1,\xe2\x80\x9d she said Tuesday, referring to South Korea\xe2\x80\x99s Sung Hyun Park, \xe2\x80\x9cI think that\xe2\x80\x99s the best experience you can get.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Augusta National officials have trumpeted their amateur invitational as a grand stage for women\xe2\x80\x99s golf. That overlooks the major originally known as the Dinah Shore, which has captivated young golfers since 1972. The tournament has cultivated indelible traditions like the winner\xe2\x80\x99s leap into Poppie\xe2\x80\x99s Pond and consistently provided significant exposure for top amateurs like Valenzuela, who used her 2016 debut here as a springboard to the Rio Olympics.'
b'Augusta National didn\xe2\x80\x99t admit its first female members until 2012. And while it has since opened its course to boys and girls through the Drive, Chip and Putt contest and to amateur women, these inclusive gestures have ignored \xe2\x80\x94 and however unintentionally, undermined \xe2\x80\x94 the L.P.G.A., one of the longest-running women\xe2\x80\x99s professional sports organizations.'
b'The Drive, Chip and Putt contest, held the Sunday before the Masters, has siphoned television and other media coverage from the final round of the ANA Inspiration. And with the advent of the Augusta National Women\xe2\x80\x99s Amateur, the spotlight on the best women\xe2\x80\x99s players in the world has become more diffuse.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think it comes from a very positive place in Augusta \xe2\x80\x94 they want to do the right thing,\xe2\x80\x9d Valenzuela said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s probably not my place to say, but it would have been great if both could have worked together to make them happen in different weeks, because you don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get the spotlight off the L.P.G.A.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The ANA Inspiration matters because the magnetic moments that attracted Valenzuela to the sport as a child didn\xe2\x80\x99t involve Augusta National, which for most of its history has been so far out of the women\xe2\x80\x99s orbit that it might as well have been on Mars.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cEver since I\xe2\x80\x99ve been a little girl I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen all the girls jump in the pond,\xe2\x80\x9d Valenzuela said, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the women\xe2\x80\x99s Masters.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'On Wednesday, Jennifer Kupcho, a Wake Forest senior and the top-ranked women\xe2\x80\x99s amateur, struck the opening drive of the new event at the Champions Retreat resort in Evans, Ga., the site of the first two rounds. Only the final round will be played at Augusta National.'
b'Across the country Wednesday, Valenzuela grooved her swing on the Mission Hills practice range. Stacy Lewis, the 2011 ANA Inspiration champion, said she planned to extend a warm welcome to Valenzuela and the four other amateurs in the field: Patty Tavatanakit, Frida Kinhult, Rachel Heck and Xin Kou, who is also known as Cindy.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m really proud of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lewis, the former women\xe2\x80\x99s world No. 1 who recently returned to competition after the birth in October of her first child, a daughter. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to play professional golf, you need to play in majors, and you need to see where your game stacks up and have that experience of playing in front of those big crowds.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Valenzuela has advanced to the weekend in each of her previous starts in this event. If she misses the cut this week, she will not be idle over the weekend. Her consolation prize will be joining her Stanford teammates on Sunday at the Silverado Showdown, a collegiate women\xe2\x80\x99s event in Napa.'
b'Stanford is ranked fourth in the Women\xe2\x80\x99s Golf Coaches Association poll and is jockeying with other top teams for seeding positions at next month\xe2\x80\x99s N.C.A.A. championship. Another Stanford star, Andrea Lee, chose to play in Georgia this week.'
b'Anne Walker, Valenzuela\xe2\x80\x99s coach at Stanford, said in a telephone interview that she welcomed Augusta National\xe2\x80\x99s move into the women\xe2\x80\x99s game, describing it as \xe2\x80\x9canother great opportunity that\xe2\x80\x99s opened up.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But in its exuberance to grow the game, Augusta National big-footed more than the L.P.G.A. schedule. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s very little discussion about the timing of it as it relates to college golf,\xe2\x80\x9d Walker said.'
b'She pointed out that Texas, which is ranked second behind the University of Southern California, competed in the Bruzzy Challenge this week in Texas without three of its top players: Emilee Hoffman, Agathe Laisne and Kaitlyn Papp, who are in Augusta. The Longhorns finished tied for seventh, their lowest finish of the season by three places.'
b'Walker ultimately described the scheduling conflicts as \xe2\x80\x9ca great problem to have.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But they are a problem, nonetheless.'
b'When the Augusta National chairman, Fred Ridley, announced the new event last year, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe think that to have one week where the future greats of the game and the current greats of the women\xe2\x80\x99s game are all competing on a big stage is, it\xe2\x80\x99s just very exciting.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Referring to L.P.G.A. Commissioner Mike Whan, who was not consulted before the Augusta tournament was scheduled, Ridley added, \xe2\x80\x9cI think he also understands that in time it\xe2\x80\x99s going to be a win for the L.P.G.A.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Lewis said she would love to see the Augusta event played at a different time of year.'
b'Referring to the amateurs, she added, \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe they could get a full tournament on Augusta National. Get a couple practice rounds and a three-round tournament there.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Or better yet, the Augusta National members could find a way to welcome the 8-year-old girls and the 18-year-old amateurs after they have grown into the best women players on the planet.'
b'It\xe2\x80\x99s a win-win for golf any time the best women mix with the best men, as Brittany Lincicome, a two-time ANA Inspiration champion, recently was reminded.'
b'Lincicome spent two days last month at the PGA Tour\xe2\x80\x99s flagship event in Florida, and she was shocked when players like Sergio Garcia and Rory McIlroy saw her behind the rope lines and came bounding over to ask if she would pose for selfies with them.'
b'Lincicome, 33, has been slow to recognize that her start last year in a PGA Tour event in Kentucky trumped either of her victorious finishes here.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI had no idea they knew who I was,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'
b'If the L.P.G.A. has to move the date of its first major to accommodate Augusta National, here\xe2\x80\x99s one idea how Augusta National members could make the change palatable. They could extend an invitation to the women\xe2\x80\x99s reigning major winners to compete alongside the men in the Masters\xe2\x80\x99 par-3 contest. That would be more meaningful start than a flashy 18-hole finish to an amateur event.'

b'By Richard Goldstein'
b'Alice Dye, who teamed with her husband, Pete, to pioneer modern golf-course architecture and worked to promote the women\xe2\x80\x99s game for more than half a century, died on Friday in Gulf Stream, Fla. She was 91.'
b'Her death was announced by Dye Designs, the family\xe2\x80\x99s company.'
b'The Dyes designed dozens of venues, in the United States and abroad, many of them regularly ranked among the best in the world and frequently chosen to host professional tournaments.'
b'That roster includes the stadium course at TPC Sawgrass, in Ponte Vedra Beach, Fla.; PGA West, near Palm Springs, Calif.; Whistling Straits, on Lake Michigan in Sheboygan, Wis.; the Ocean Course, on Kiawah Island in South Carolina; Harbour Town Golf Links, in Hilton Head, S.C.; and Crooked Stick, in Alice\xe2\x80\x99s hometown, Indianapolis.'
b'Their courses were generally known as Pete Dye designs, but Alice provided significant input, and her husband usually took her advice.'
b'Their signature hole was the 17th at TPC Sawgrass, the home of the Players Championship. When Pete was unsure how to fill in sandy terrain he had hollowed out around the green for transfer to other spots on the course, Alice provided the solution.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cOriginally, the water was just supposed to come into play on the right side, but we just kept digging,\xe2\x80\x9d the Golf Channel quoted Mr. Dye as saying. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then one day Alice came out and said, \xe2\x80\x98Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you just go ahead and make it an island?\xe2\x80\x99 So we did.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'That green, connected to the rest of the course by a slender land bridge, has tormented even the world\xe2\x80\x99s greatest golfers and become one of the most recognized images in the sport.'
b'When they were building the Ocean Course on Kiawah Island in 1990, Ms. Dye persuaded her husband to raise the fairways to harmonize them with the environment.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cPete, I can\xe2\x80\x99t see the ocean on the back nine,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as related by The New York Times. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t just want to hear it; I want to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mr. Dye raised the fairways by six feet so that the ocean came into view. But that created an added challenge by exposing the course to unpredictable, sometimes strong winds.'
b'Both husband and wife were avid golfers, but Ms. Dye was the more accomplished of the two; she won some 50 amateur titles as well as two United States Golf Association senior championships and two Canadian senior championships. She played on the United States\xe2\x80\x99 victorious Curtis Cup team in 1970.'
b'In 1983, Ms. Dye was the first woman admitted to the American Society of Golf Course Architects. In 1997, she became the first woman to serve as the society\xe2\x80\x99s president. She received the PGA of America\xe2\x80\x99s First Lady of Golf Award in 2004.'
b'Ms. Dye championed forward tees that make formidable courses more playable for most women as well as for male players outside the pro ranks.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI worked very hard on trying to get the two-tee system for women,\xe2\x80\x9d she told the PGA of America. \xe2\x80\x9cI was successful in getting the yardage down between 5,000 and 5,200 yards.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re total opposites,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Dye said when she and Mr. Dye were interviewed by PBS in 2012. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s artistic and I\xe2\x80\x99m the practical one.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'On her death, Ron Whitten wrote in Golf Digest: \xe2\x80\x9cShe was the more successful competitive golfer, with a supple swing. She was a better politician than Pete when it came to dealing with owners and regulators, more polished in presentations and communications. As a golf architect, she was the more knowledgeable of the two, teaching Pete how to read contour maps and handling most of his drawings.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In his memoir, \xe2\x80\x9cBury Me in a Pot Bunker\xe2\x80\x9d (1995, with Mark Shaw), Pete Dye wrote of his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cShe may have taken a back seat to whatever publicity I\xe2\x80\x99ve had over the last 30 years, but everyone in the golf industry knows how important her contributions have been, since she has such an intuition for what makes a golf course challenging but playable.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Alice O\xe2\x80\x99Neal was born in Indianapolis on Feb. 19, 1927, to Perry and Lucy (Holliday) O\xe2\x80\x99Neal. Her father, a corporate lawyer, was an avid golfer.'
b'Alice developed her smooth swing at a local country club junior class and began winning tournaments as a teenager.'
b'She met Mr. Dye when they were students at Rollins College, in Winter Park, Fla., where she was captain of the women\xe2\x80\x99s golf team and also played on the men\xe2\x80\x99s squad along with Pete. Alice graduated in 1948, but Pete dropped out. They were married in 1950 and settled in Indianapolis.'
b'The couple were selling insurance there in the mid-1950s when they decided to pursue golf-course design. They advanced their knowledge of the profession when they toured Britain in 1963 and visited numerous courses.'
b'Alice Dye is survived by her husband; their sons, Perry and Paul Burke (known as P.B.) Dye; two grandchildren; and two great-grandchildren. Both sons are golf architects with Dye Designs.'
b'Ms. Dye looked back on her career in a book of her own, \xe2\x80\x9cFrom Birdies to Bunkers: Discover How Golf Can Bring Love, Humor, and Success Into Your Life\xe2\x80\x9d (2004), also written with Mr. Shaw.'
b'Into her 80s, she offered insights for women who play golf.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBecause they have to tee off last on so many holes, a lot of women get self-conscious and worry that everyone is analyzing or judging their golf swing,\xe2\x80\x9d she told The Times in 2012. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not true. They might be looking at their shoes or their hat. Most men don\xe2\x80\x99t know enough about the golf swing to analyze it.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'LONDON \xe2\x80\x94 President Trump has implied that a golf course owned by the Trump Organization in Scotland helps cement the United States\xe2\x80\x99 relationship with Britain, just a few days after a Scottish court ruled that the company had to pay expenses in a failed lawsuit.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cVery proud of perhaps the greatest golf course anywhere in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Trump wrote on Twitter on Saturday, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cAlso, furthers U.K. relationship!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Very proud of perhaps the greatest golf course anywhere in the world. Also, furthers U.K. relationship! https://t.co/3xTzzJH6Iq'
b'The Trump International golf course near Aberdeen, in northeastern Scotland, was ordered on Wednesday to cover the Scottish government\xe2\x80\x99s legal costs after the company was unsuccessful in its attempts to halt a North Sea wind power development.'
b'Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s company had argued that the wind turbines would mar the view from the golf course. The case went to the Supreme Court of the United Kingdom in December 2015, where the company lost, setting up the ruling about who should pay costs.'
b'The exact sum of the reimbursement that the Trump Organization now has to pay has not been disclosed. The White House did not immediately respond to a request for comment.'
b'The president\xe2\x80\x99s tweet prompted bafflement and concern among some legal and ethics experts.'
b'Thomas Lundmark, a professor of law at the University of Hull, in northern England, said in a telephone interview on Monday that the tweet \xe2\x80\x9cmade me curious.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what he\xe2\x80\x99s talking about,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Lundmark added. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes it further a relationship for him? For the United States? For us? How?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Though the ruling in Scotland was against Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s company, Mr. Lundmark suggested that there were constitutional and ethical concerns.'
b'The American Constitution prohibits the president from accepting payments from foreign and domestic governments. Mr. Trump owns golf courses, hotels and other properties that are frequented by foreign and domestic government officials.'
b'Some have claimed that Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s promotion of his properties while president amount to a violation of the emoluments clauses in the Constitution.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s turned companies over to other people to manage, but he is still a beneficiary,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Lundmark said.'
b'John Linarelli, an American professor of commercial law at Durham University in northeast England, said that there was \xe2\x80\x9cplausible concern\xe2\x80\x9d that the constitutional rules were being violated.'
b'It was also, he added, \xe2\x80\x9ca question of ethics.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou want him to have our interests in mind when he\xe2\x80\x99s managing foreign policy \xe2\x80\x94 not his own,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Linarelli added.'
b'Walter M. Shaub Jr., who resigned his post as the United States government\xe2\x80\x99s top ethics watchdog in July 2017, said in a tweet on Saturday that Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s comment was the president\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cmost explicit commingling of personal interests and public office to date.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThis is shameless, corrupt and repugnant presidential profiteering,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is an invitation to graft.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mr. Shaub is now a senior adviser for Citizens for Responsibility and Ethics in Washington, a nonprofit advocacy group.'
b'The group sued Mr. Trump in January 2017, arguing that he was \xe2\x80\x9cillegally receiving payments from foreign governments.\xe2\x80\x9d The lawsuit cleared a hurdle last March when a federal judge in Maryland refused the Justice Department\xe2\x80\x99s request to dismiss it.'
b'In July, another lawsuit accusing the president of violating the Constitution by maintaining a financial interest in his company\xe2\x80\x99s Washington hotel was allowed to move to the evidence-gathering stage.'

b"AUGUSTA Ga. (Reuters) - Rory McIlroy will begin his latest quest to complete the career grand slam of golf's four majors when he tees off alongside American Rickie Fowler and Australia's Cameron Smith at the Masters starting on Thursday at Augusta National."
b"It will be McIlroy's fifth attempt at the Masters to complete his collection of major titles and is perhaps his best chance yet as the Northern Irishman enters the week as the hottest player on the planet."
b"McIlroy's grouping, which will be the 15th of 29 to take the course, will start their round at 11:15 a.m. ET (1515 GMT) and follow the threesome of four-times champion Tiger Woods, China's Li Haotong and Spaniard Jon Rahm around the course."
b"Looking to join Gene Sarazen, Ben Hogan, Gary Player, Jack Nicklaus and Woods as winners of the career grand slam, McIlroy enters the year's first major in top form."
b'With a top-six finish in each of the six stroke play events he has played this year, including a triumph at The Players Championship three weeks ago, McIlroy is a popular pick to make amends for his final-round letdown last year.'
b'McIlroy, whose grouping will be the last out on Friday at 2 p.m. ET behind the Woods threesome, started three shots back of eventual champion Patrick Reed entering the final round in 2018 but closed with two-over 74 to finish in a share of fifth place.'
b'Three-times champion Phil Mickelson will play the opening two rounds alongside 2013 U.S. Open champion Justin Rose and 2017 PGA Championship winner Justin Thomas in the penultimate grouping on Thursday.'

b'World number two Dustin Johnson will play the opening two rounds with Bryson DeChambeau and Australian Jason Day while reigning champion Patrick Reed will play alongside fellow American Webb Simpson and Norwegian amateur Viktor Hovland.'

b'AUGUSTA Ga. (Reuters) - Bryson DeChambeau has earned the "mad scientist" nickname for his calculated approach to golf and may need to spend some extra time in his quirky laboratory to find a winning formula at this week\'s Masters.'
b'DeChambeau, a physics major who previously used a since-banned compass to help read greens, has five wins on the PGA Tour but taming Augusta National will prove the toughest test yet for the American world number six.'
b"The year's first major does not provide players with green-reading books, and that could put DeChambeau well out of his comfort zone given the highly-contoured greens at Augusta National that can frustrate the game's best putters."
b'"I have to work a little harder to get some insight into some things than other places, but that\'s fine," DeChambeau told reporters on Monday.'
b'"I mean, that\'s a part of the process, and I think the person who digs it out of the dirt the most should have a little bit of an advantage and I think that\'s where it\'s actually a positive thing."'
b"DeChambeau, who finished as the low amateur at the Masters in 2016 before joining the PGA Tour the following year, already has a win this season but knows he faces a difficult challenge going into Thursday's opening round."
b'Unlike many of his competitors, DeChambeau will not be able to rely on the detailed notes they have modified over the years after logging countless practice and tournament rounds at Augusta National.'
b'DeChambeau made no secrets when asked about what impact the lack of green-reading books could have on him this week.'
b'"Now I practiced trying to understand what one percent is, what two percent is, based on my eyes," said DeChambeau. "Is it as precise as the greens books? Absolutely not. We still have to feel and sense with our eyes what it\'s going to do.'
b'"That\'s really all I can do and I have to practice a lot more hitting breaking putts, because I can\'t just bring out my compass and go, oh, it\'s 3 percent and here it is. I have to look at and walk around and go, okay, I\'m acclimated to 3 percent."'
b' (Reporting by Frank Pingue, editing by Pritha Sarkar)'

b'By The Associated Press'
b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 Phil Mickelson flashed the faraway look of a man approaching a half-century of life, clearly pondering where the time went.'
b'"It goes by fast," he reminisced, glancing toward the rolling hills and towering pines beyond the Augusta National clubhouse.'
b'Then, just like that, Lefty snapped out of it.'
b"He's got his sights on a fourth green jacket."
b'"I think I\'ve got another major in me," Mickelson said confidently. "At least one, maybe two. I would love to get one right here."'
b"The 100th round of his Masters career wasn't all that memorable, a middling 1-over 73 Friday in which he was forced to scramble mightily to overcome a wayward driver."
b"But it was enough to keep him in the thick of things after an opening-round 67, to give him a shot at becoming the oldest player to win one of golf's major championships."
b'For a sport that can be a bit lethargic, needlessly complicated and way too stuffy, this remains one of its overwhelming charms.'
b"On any given day, the old guys can still go swing-for-swing with all those buffed-up youngsters, giving us all a bit of hope as we approach middle age and beyond, clear evidence that our golden years don't have to be confined to a rocking chair."
b"That's especially the case at Augusta National, which embraces the geezers like a warm, comfy blanket."
b'"Every time I come here, I just feel like a kid again," Mickelson said. "It\'s so much fun."'
b"He's already got one major title since entering his 40s, capturing the 2013 British Open at age 43. Now, with his 49th birthday a little over two months away, there's little doubt that he's still got the game to win another."
b'"I expected to be a little bit better, to be honest," said Mickelson, whose 4-under 140 left him three shots off the lead. "But there\'s nothing better than having a chance going into the weekend at the Masters, and that\'s what I want to focus on. I know that I\'m playing well enough."'
b"As with all sports, golf will always be a young man's game."
b'A player is far more likely to win a major title in his 20s than his 40s.'
b"But there's a good three dozen members of the Over-40 Major Champions Club, and it seems only a matter of time before someone in their 50s break through to win one of the biggest events."
b'For now, the oldest to do it was Julius Boros, at the PGA Championship way back in 1968, when he was about four months past his 48th birthday. But Tom Watson very nearly captured the 2009 British Open at age 59, squandering a lead with a bogey on the 72nd hole and losing to Stewart Cink in a four-hole playoff.'
b"Mickelson already has a victory this season and it's not at all far-fetched to envision him contending for a few more championships after he turns 49 on June 16 \xe2\x80\x94 which just happens to be the same day as the final round of the U.S. Open at Pebble Beach, site of his most recent triumph a mere two months ago."
b'The Masters leaderboard also included a pair of 43-year-olds, Tiger Woods and Ian Poulter. But, really, the most impressive performance of the first two days was turned in by Bernhard Langer, the 61-year-old German who is graying at the edges but still looks as fit as he did in his prime.'
b"Langer isn't going to win the tournament. But he easily made the cut, cruising around the course he knows so well with rounds of 71 and 72. He can't hit it as far as those players half as age (and then some), but he knows each nook and cranny of the course, the sort of local knowledge that goes far at a place such as this."
b"Not that he'd turn down a dip in the fountain of youth."
b'"I\'m hitting a 4-iron and (younger players are) hitting a 9-iron," Langer quipped. "I would rather hit a 9-iron and have a little less experience."'
b"He closed out Friday's round with three birdies on the last five holes, putting him one shot ahead of 25-year-old Jordan Spieth and ensuring he would finish above the cut line for the fifth time in the last seven years."
b'But Father Time will catch him one of these days.'
b'Just ask Ian Woosnam, the diminutive Welshman who won the Masters in 1991, the same year Mickelson made his Augusta debut as an amateur from Arizona State.'
b"Sharing Langer's age but plagued by a bad back, Woosnam is no match for this course. Hasn't been for the better part of two decades. He wanted to quit a few years ago, but his spouse wouldn't let him."
b'"I got a telling off from my wife," Woosnam joked. "She said, \'Get out there and do it again."'
b"But Friday's round was his last at Augusta National."
b'If Woosnam comes back again, it will just be for fun. After rounds of 80 and 76 \xe2\x80\x94 and missing the cut for the 11th year in a row \xe2\x80\x94 he announced his Masters retirement as soon as he walked off the 18th green.'
b'"It doesn\'t seem to get any better," Woosnam said, in words that everyone can relate to with the passing of the years. "Maybe if they will give me a cart or something like that, I\'ll come back. That would be all right. But I don\'t think I\'ll get that."'
b'Woosnam savored the laughs and the memories.'
b'This part of his journey was done.'
b'In the end, the years always win.'
b'But in golf, sometimes, it takes them longer to catch you.'

b'AUGUSTA Ga. (Reuters) - Brooks Koepka was all brawn in the first round of the Masters but on Friday he was all brains as he showcased his mental toughness on a day when it looked like he might drop out of contention.'
b'A day following a flawless trip around Augusta National, overnight leader Koepka was forced to battle through ups-and-downs en route to a one-under-par 71 that left him in a five-way share of a one-shot lead after the second round.'
b'"I\'m proud of myself the way I hung in there," said Koepka, who is on seven-under-par for the tournament. "To be where I\'m at, as badly as I putted and hit the ball today, I\'m pleased with it."'
b'Three-time major champion Koepka birdied the first hole but looked like he might unravel after running into trouble at the par-five second where he made a double-bogey after his drive found the trees to the left and second shot went in a hazard.'
b'Koepka responded immediately with a birdie but then had a pair of bogeys over the next three holes, at which point it appeared the new leaders would begin to pull away on an Augusta National layout that was softened due to rain.'
b'But Koepka righted the ship with a birdie at the par-five eighth and then played solid golf on the back nine with birdies at 15 and 18, the latter which will surely send him into the night feeling better.'
b'"I know some people don\'t think I\'m mentally tough, or tough in general, but I think I am," said Koepka. "I\'ve proven that with three (major) trophies.'

b'"I\'m not trying to, you know\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x91\xe2\x80\x91 just because it\'s a major on a Saturday, I\'m not going to go out and do anything different. Just go tee it up, look where I want to hit it and fire at it."'

b"AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 The Mexican flag has been waving proudly at Augusta National with amateur Alvaro Ortiz taking the baton from Maria Fassi and carrying it into this week's Masters with his eyes on a Green Jacket."
b'After a four-decade absence, Augusta National is getting a fresh taste of Latin flair served up by a new generation of Mexican golfers primed to battle for titles.'
b"Fassi provided Ortiz with a tough act to follow by finishing runner-up to Jennifer Kupcho in the inaugural Augusta National Women's Amateur after a stirring back-nine battle with the American on Saturday."
b'Now Ortiz steps into the spotlight as he becomes the first player from his country since Victor Regalado in 1979 to play at the Masters and only the second Mexican amateur following Juan Antonio Estrada who featured at Augusta from 1962-64.'
b'The determined Ortiz finished runner-up in the two previous Latin America Amateur Championship tournaments before this year claiming the title and his Masters invite at the third attempt.'
b'"It was a huge deal for my family and me for sure and the whole country," said Ortiz, who will turn professional immediately after the Masters. "When I came back to Mexico after winning, it was all cheers and people were so happy.'
b'"People were so proud that I was going to be able to put a Mexican flag up there."'
b"Ortiz said he had received plenty of advice ahead of Thursday's opening round with most people telling him to relax and enjoy the experience."
b'But the 23-year-old, showing no fear and more than a little cockiness, said he would be trying to win a Green Jacket like everyone else, although admitting if given a chance to sign for a top 10 right now he would take it.'
b'"In my mind, I\'m out here to compete and I want the Green Jacket," he said. "I think every player is thinking like that.'
b'"Of course, winning the low amateur would be nice," he said referring to the Silver Cup awarded to the lowest amateur to make the cut.'
b'"Making the cut would be a really good finish too but if you give me a top 10 right now, I\'ll sign it for sure."'
b'While a top 10 finish would guarantee a Masters invite next year, Ortiz is soaking up every moment of his experience now.'

b"AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 The Mexican flag has been waving proudly at Augusta National with amateur Alvaro Ortiz taking the baton from Maria Fassi and carrying it into this week's Masters with his eyes on a Green Jacket."
b'After a four-decade absence, Augusta National is getting a fresh taste of Latin flair served up by a new generation of Mexican golfers primed to battle for titles.'
b"Fassi provided Ortiz with a tough act to follow by finishing runner-up to Jennifer Kupcho in the inaugural Augusta National Women's Amateur after a stirring back-nine battle with the American on Saturday."
b'Now Ortiz steps into the spotlight as he becomes the first player from his country since Victor Regalado in 1979 to play at the Masters and only the second Mexican amateur following Juan Antonio Estrada who featured at Augusta from 1962-64.'
b'The determined Ortiz finished runner-up in the two previous Latin America Amateur Championship tournaments before this year claiming the title and his Masters invite at the third attempt.'
b'"It was a huge deal for my family and me for sure and the whole country," said Ortiz, who will turn professional immediately after the Masters. "When I came back to Mexico after winning, it was all cheers and people were so happy.'
b'"People were so proud that I was going to be able to put a Mexican flag up there."'
b"Ortiz said he had received plenty of advice ahead of Thursday's opening round with most people telling him to relax and enjoy the experience."
b'But the 23-year-old, showing no fear and more than a little cockiness, said he would be trying to win a Green Jacket like everyone else, although admitting if given a chance to sign for a top 10 right now he would take it.'
b'"In my mind, I\'m out here to compete and I want the Green Jacket," he said. "I think every player is thinking like that.'
b'"Of course, winning the low amateur would be nice," he said referring to the Silver Cup awarded to the lowest amateur to make the cut.'
b'"Making the cut would be a really good finish too but if you give me a top 10 right now, I\'ll sign it for sure."'
b'While a top 10 finish would guarantee a Masters invite next year, Ortiz is soaking up every moment of his experience now.'
b"He will spend Monday night in the Crow's Nest above the Augusta National clubhouse, where amateurs have traditionally slept, and promised to bite into the local delicacy -- a pimento cheese sandwich -- at some point. "
b'"Yeah, everybody was talking about that," Ortiz said with a smile. "Actually, I received more arguments about the... chicken barbecue sandwich, which I think is pretty good. I already tried that one, but I\'ll have to try the pimento cheese."'

b"AUGUSTA, Georgia \xe2\x80\x94 When Justin Rose launches his 14th bid for Masters glory this week he will be armed with expert knowledge of Augusta National's notoriously tricky greens and something perhaps even more valuable -- a good friend. "
b"After three months apart, the newly re-minted world number one told reporters on Monday that he was pleased to be reunited with long-time caddie Mark 'Fooch' Fulcher, who had to take time off following heart surgery in January."
b'"I feel like we\'ve learned together so much here, I wouldn\'t expect a nugget of information I don\'t know to come out during practice," Rose said after a practice round.'
b"He added that Fulcher's presence would be especially valuable in the later rounds of the tournament, when emotions run high."
b'"It\'s when the going gets tough," Rose said. "We have so many positive experiences out there under pressure that we can draw on together."'
b'The 2013 U.S. Open champion has clinched a top-10 finish on five separate occasions at The Masters. '
b'But perhaps no single outing at Augusta National was more formative to Rose than his devastating playoff loss to Spaniard Sergio Garcia in 2017.'
b'"I felt like that was the first major I\'ve been close to and not won, really, like a heartbreaker," Rose said. "I think I took comfort in the fact that you can\'t get through a career without something like that happening."'

b'By The Associated Press'
b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 They crowded hundreds deep around the 18th green, roaring when those lucky enough to be in front signaled the first sight of Tiger Woods in his familiar red coming up the fairway.'
b'Up by the clubhouse, the kitchen help stood in white jackets and hats, much like a previous generation did all those years ago. In front of them, a group of young people working this week as trash pickers tried unsuccessfully to find a piece of higher ground to witness history.'
b'Few in the huge throng had any hope of seeing anything. But they could hear, and on this day that was enough.'
b'Off to the side of the green, the Woods children had a much better view. His daughter, Sam, was a year old when dad won his last major championship and son, Charlie, had yet to be born.'
b'They had watched last summer as Woods tried desperately to break his major championship drought at the British Open, only to come up just short.'
b'"I wasn\'t going to let that happen to them twice," Woods said.'
b"On a magical afternoon at Augusta National, there was no chance of that happening. Woods didn't let them or any of his millions of fans around the world down on a day that will live in Masters lore, already thick with Woods' achievements."
b"This wasn't his signature win from 22 years ago, when he was the young phenom who thrilled the country by romping to victory for the first time at Augusta National."
b'But it seemed just as good, just as satisfying, both to him and the massive galleries that did all they could to try and will him to a win.'
b'"To come back here and play as well as I did and did all the things \xe2\x80\x94 all the little things well this week, and to do it here," Woods said, choking up at the thought. "This has meant so much to me and my family, this tournament, and to have everyone here, it\'s something I\'ll never, ever forget.'
b"At the age of 43, he's a major champion again after fighting his way through a leaderboard filled with young talent, most who list Woods as a golf hero. He seized control of the tournament with near kick-in birdies on the 15th and 16th holes."
b'That got him his fifth green jacket, but this meant more. So much more.'
b'All the doubt. All the doubters.'
b'All the issues no one ever expected Woods to have.'
b'"I don\'t know of a better comeback in sports in my lifetime," former champion Zach Johnson said.'
b'America got up early to watch Woods try and win a major from behind for the first time, after tee times were moved up nearly six hours because of threatening weather.'
b'And America got quite a show.'
b"Woods grabbed the lead in Amen Corner when everyone around him seemed to be hitting balls into Rae's Creek. Then he held on for dear life as the birdies started pouring in from those playing in front of him."
b'"Now I know why I\'m balding," Woods said. "This stuff is hard."'
b'The key to his remarkable day, though, might have come after a 3-putt on the fifth hole that left him three shots behind Francesco Molinari, who had beaten him at the British Open in front of his kids.'
b'Caddie Joe LaCava had seen enough, and gave Woods a talking too on the way to the sixth hole.'
b'"He was saying some things that I can\'t really repeat here," Woods said. "Then I went into the restroom and proceeded to say the same things over and over to myself. And then came out and I felt a lot better."'
b'Woods called it a grind, and it was until his tee shot on the par-3 16th hole almost went in. It became a coronation on the final holes, as roars echoed with each birdie and the inevitability of it all started to sink in.'
b'The crowd was thrilled, even those who had to listen to figure out what was happening out of sight. So, it seems, was almost every sports hero around the world.'
b"Tom Brady was watching from his couch and tweeted his congratulations. Serena Williams took a break from partying at fellow tennis player Caroline Wozniacki's bachelorette party to see it all unfold."
b'"I am literally in tears watching," Williams tweeted . "This is Greatness like no other."'
b'President Donald Trump and former President Barack Obama weighed in, and so did Steph Curry, calling it the greatest comeback in sports. At the Orioles game in Baltimore they played a highlight of Woods on the 18th green during a break between innings as the crowd cheered.'
b"Woods himself screamed out in sheer joy after sinking his final putt, and no one could blame him. It had been a long, tortured path back to the top and the way it all ended at the place that he loves best couldn't have been scripted in a Hollywood movie."
b'It had been 14 long years since his last Masters win. Eleven years since his last major championship.'
b'And now the question becomes not can Woods win again, but whether he can beat the record of 18 major championships set by Jack Nicklaus.'
b'"I really haven\'t thought about that yet," Woods said. "I\'m sure that I\'ll probably think of it going down the road. But right now, it\'s a little soon, and I\'m just enjoying 15."'
b"So was LaCava, who signed on to Woods's bag at a time where he was still dealing with back issues and a messy DUI and wasn't sure he would even play again. LaCava has been on the bag to see Woods change both as a player and a person."
b'"He\'s high-fiving people, he\'s signing some autographs," LaCava said. "He\'s much more fan-friendly, which is awesome."'
b'The two fans Woods wanted to win for most were in his family.'
b'They got hugs from dad on the green, then walked with him through an opening in the crowd as chants of "Tiger, Tiger, Tiger" rang out.'
b'"I hope they\'re proud of their dad," Woods said.'
b"Surely they are. But they're not alone."

b'By The Associated Press'
b'LINCOLN, Neb. \xe2\x80\x94 Noor Ahmed outwardly lives her Muslim faith, and even growing up in a state as diverse as California she says she encountered hostility on the street, in school and on the golf course.'
b'One of the top junior golfers in Northern California coming out of high school, Ahmed was a starter in her first year at Nebraska and the No. 2 player most of this spring. She is believed to be the only golfer at the college level or higher who competes in a hijab, the headscarf worn in adherence to the Muslim faith.'
b"Arriving in Lincoln two years ago, Ahmed sensed hesitancy from teammates mostly from small Midwestern towns and unaccustomed to seeing a woman in a hijab. She didn't feel embraced until an unfortunate yet unifying event roiled the campus midway through her freshman year."
b'A video surfaced of a student claiming to be the "most active white nationalist in the Nebraska area," disparaging minorities and advocating violence. The student, it turned out, was in the same biology lecture class as Ahmed.'
b"Teammates offered to walk with her across campus, and one who would become her best friend, Kate Smith, invited Ahmed to stay with her. She didn't accept but was heartened by the gesture."
b'"That," Smith said, "was when she realized how much each and every one of us care for her on the team, that it wasn\'t just like, \'Hey you\'re our teammate.\' No, it\'s \'We want you to be safe, we want you to feel at home here.\'"'
b'Having grown up in the post-9/11 era, Ahmed, like many Muslims in the United States, has been a target for bullying and verbal abuse. She began wearing the hijab in middle school.'
b'On the course, in an airport or even walking across campus she can feel the long stares and notices the glances. She said she has never been physically threatened \xe2\x80\x94 "that I know of" \xe2\x80\x94 and that most of the face-to-face insults came before she arrived at Nebraska.'
b"Much of the venom spewed at her now comes on social media. She has been the subject of several media profiles, and each sparks another round of hateful messages. She acknowledges she reads but doesn't respond to messages and that an athletic department sports psychologist has helped her learn how to deal with them."
b'"I\'ve been called every racial slur in the book," she said. "I\'ve been told explicitly that people who look like me don\'t play golf, we don\'t have a right to exist in America, you should go home. It would definitely faze me a little bit, but it never deterred me. I\'m really stubborn, so I\'m going to prove you wrong, just wait. When people think they\'re dragging me down, it kind of fuels the fire in me that I\'m going to be a better golfer, I\'m going to be a better student, I\'m going to keep climbing up the ladder."'
b'The daughter of Egyptian immigrants is from a close-knit family in Folsom, California, and she steeled herself for the cultural adjustment she would have to make at Nebraska.'
b"She dealt with loneliness and anxiety, especially her freshman year. She had difficulty finding a support network. There is a small Muslim community on campus, but she didn't immerse herself in it. The demands on athletes are great, and they are largely segregated, eating and studying in facilities separate from those used by regular students."
b'Nebraska coach Robin Krapfl said she was initially concerned about how teammates would react to Ahmed. Krapfl remembered meeting with her golfers and telling them about her.'
b'"I could tell by a couple of the looks and maybe even a comment or two that they weren\'t 100 percent comfortable with that," Krapfl said. "A lot of our girls come from small-town communities that are very limited in their ethnicity. It\'s just the fear of the unknown. They had just never been exposed to being around someone from the Muslim faith."'
b'Krapfl said she saw a golfer or two roll their eyes, another shook her head. "I overheard, \'Why would Coach bring someone like that on the team?\' "'
b'"Luckily when she got here people could see her for who she was and the quality of person she was," Krapfl said. "It took a while. It really did. You\'ve got to get to know somebody, who they really are and not just what they look like."'
b'Smith said she sometimes cringes when she and Ahmed are in a group and the conversation turns to politics, immigration or even fashion, like when someone innocently or ignorantly tells Ahmed that she would look good in a short dress or a certain hairstyle.'
b'"She can never wear a short dress, so why would you want to depict her as that?" Smith said. "You have to respect her beliefs and why she\'s doing it. Also, I think a lot of things are connected to women\'s beauty standards and how people don\'t think she can look beautiful when she\'s covered. I think she\'s a really beautiful girl no matter how much skin she\'s showing."'
b'For all the challenges Ahmed faced, there have been positives. Some people have complimented her for living her faith as she sees fit, a Muslim teen who golfs in a hijab and lives in the United Kingdom wrote to says she draws inspiration from her, and a player for another college team approached her at an event to tell her she recently converted to Islam and just wanted to say hi.'
b'"I remember going and crying and, wow, I\'m not alone out here," she said.'
b"Ahmed said she's naturally shy and a bit uncomfortable with the attention, but she hopes Muslim girls coming up behind her are watching."
b'"I grew up never seeing anyone like me," she said. "Honestly, I didn\'t realize how much grief I was carrying, having never seen an image of myself or someone who looked like me in popular American culture. It\'s a big deal.'
b'"Why are basketball and football so heavily African American? If I were black and I saw people who looked like me competing in that sport, that\'s probably the sport I would choose. I think it\'s really important when we\'re talking about trying to make golf and other sports and other areas in American culture diverse, how important it is to see someone who looks like you and how it will fuel other people\'s interest."'
b'Ahmed started playing golf at 8, and her parents encouraged her to take the sport to the highest level possible. Wearing the hijab has never interfered with her game and she has never considered not wearing it on the course.'
b'"I think Muslim women who choose to observe it or choose not to observe it have the right to exist in any space they want to be in," she said, "and I would feel like I would be sending a message that the hijab doesn\'t exist in this place or it shouldn\'t, and I don\'t feel comfortable with that."'

b'By Karen Crouse'
b'PONTE VEDRA BEACH, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 At last week\xe2\x80\x99s Arnold Palmer Invitational, Phil Mickelson carded a 68 in the first round while chewing gum. The next day, without gum, he sprayed his shots all over the Bay Hill course and shot a 78.'
b'A coincidence? Mickelson thinks not.'
b'To boost his cognitive functioning, Mickelson, 48, started chewing gum during competition in January at the Desert Classic \xe2\x80\x94 and tied for second. Two starts later, chewing gum as he walked, Mickelson clinched his 44th PGA Tour title.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe chewing aspect stimulates the frontal cortex,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mickelson, who couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get his putter activated Thursday in the opening round of the Players Championship. Mickelson, the 2007 champion, had a four-putt from 25 feet on the par-3 third hole, which was his 12th of the round, for a triple-bogey 6 on his way to a two-over-par 74. That left him nine strokes behind the leaders, Tommy Fleetwood and Keegan Bradley, who each posted a 65.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t help much today,\xe2\x80\x9d Mickelson said with a rueful laugh. But, to the amusement of his peers, Mickelson has no plans to give up the gum.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him chomping on that gum, and I was wondering what he was doing,\xe2\x80\x9d Steve Stricker said. \xe2\x80\x9cLeave it to Phil, I guess, to come up with that.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There is some actual science to Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s assertions. Studies have shown that chewing gum is associated with improved alertness and the ability to process new information, though the results are far from definitive. A 2011 study by psychologists at St. Lawrence University found that students who chewed gum and then discarded it performed demanding cognitive tasks like repeating numbers backward and solving complex logic puzzles better than those who did not chew gum.'
b'Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s caddie and younger brother, Tim, said he could not speak to the science of gum chewing, but he could vouch for Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s improved focus. Last year, he said, Mickelson on occasion would ask him to pick a club for him to hit because he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t gather his thoughts. An inability to focus hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been an issue this year, Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s brother said.'
b'In the copycat world of professional golf, where players have turned their searching minds to mouth guards, tobacco chewing and copper bracelets as performance aids, could Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s success this year spawn other gum chewers?'
b'Any players tempted to make like Violet Beauregarde, the \xe2\x80\x9cCharlie and the Chocolate Factory\xe2\x80\x9d character who obsessively worked the same piece of gum for months at a time, should consider themselves warned. Serge Onyper, who co-wrote the St. Lawrence University study, noted in an email on Wednesday that those who chewed gum for five minutes before getting rid of it benefited compared with those who did not chew gum at all. But, he wrote, \xe2\x80\x9cthose who chewed the gum throughout the cognitive tasks did not benefit compared to those who did not chew gum.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mickelson, 48, said he chews two pieces per round, but would not divulge the type or brand. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not on the market yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'Gum comes with and without sugar, with caffeine and, now, infused with cannabidiol, or CBD, which has been used to treat a variety of symptoms, including joint inflammation. It is categorized by the tour as a dietary supplement and is not prohibited, though players are advised to use extreme caution when using it and other supplements because, in general, there is no guarantee that what\xe2\x80\x99s on the label is what\xe2\x80\x99s in the product.'
b'Tiger Woods noted that Michael Jordan chewed gum when he played basketball and said he has chewed gum on occasion for a quick sugar boost. There have been other gum chewers in the game, notably Payne Stewart, a three-time major winner. In the 1980s, the golfer Hubert Green bestowed upon Tim Norris the nickname Pac-Man because he chomped gum the way the arcade game character gobbled ghosts.'
b'Paul Azinger, NBC\xe2\x80\x99s lead golf analyst, described Norris, the 1982 Greater Hartford Open champion, as \xe2\x80\x9cthe greatest gum chewer in the history of golf,\xe2\x80\x9d a distinction that made Norris chuckle when it was recently relayed to him.'
b'Norris described his gum chewing as \xe2\x80\x9ca nervous habit\xe2\x80\x9d and said he preferred the spearmint flavor \xe2\x80\x9cbecause it was green, and if I did accidentally drop it, it blended into the grass.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'After Norris left competitive golf and embarked on a college coaching career, \xe2\x80\x9cI kind of grew out of the habit,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'Billy Horschel, the 2014 FedEx Cup champion, was forced to curb his gum habit recently when he started wearing a clear aligner on his upper teeth. Horschel described his gum chewing as \xe2\x80\x9ckind of a superstitious thing\xe2\x80\x9d but said it served a purpose.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI could chew on it harder when I needed to work out some frustrations,\xe2\x80\x9d he said Thursday after signing for a 69.'
b'Some players never stuck with gum chewing long enough for it to become habit-forming. It was not calming for Bubba Watson, who said, \xe2\x80\x9cI tried to do it to take my mind off things going on but I bit my tongue too much.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'And Stricker said, \xe2\x80\x9cI started chewing gum and all of the sudden I was building up tension in my jaws because I started really grinding on it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In 2017, Jordan Spieth chewed gum on his way to winning the British Open for his third major title.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI did it for no intended reason,\xe2\x80\x9d said Spieth, whose swing coach, Cameron McCormick, offered him a piece of gum before his opening tee shot. He got off to a quick start and just kept chomping on it until after he was done with his post-round news conference.'
b'Spieth hasn\xe2\x80\x99t made gum chewing a habit, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think there\xe2\x80\x99s something to it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI could sit here and say Phil\xe2\x80\x99s blowing smoke but this one makes sense. When you\xe2\x80\x99re focused on chewing, that can take your mind off of golf and can kind of calm players a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'If Mickelson is so sold on gum\xe2\x80\x99s cognitive benefits, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he chew his standard two pieces during the second round of the Arnold Palmer Invitational, where he ended up missing the cut?'

b'CAPE TOWN \xe2\x80\x94 Clifford van der Venter admits his own golf game is \xe2\x80\x9cpretty useless.\xe2\x80\x9d Nevertheless, he was drawn to one of South Africa\xe2\x80\x99s most prestigious golf estates, stretching over 2,500 rolling acres with a Jack Nicklaus signature course, two full-size polo fields, three gyms and a winery.'
b'Grapevines flank the boulevards. Zebra and antelope nibble grass on a small reserve.'
b'After a fast-paced career in the corporate world, Mr. van der Venter, 54, enjoys strolling through the grounds of Val de Vie, a gated community 35 miles north of Cape Town that has been judged South Africa\xe2\x80\x99s best for the past two years by New World Wealth, a market research group based in Johannesburg. He also feels extremely safe there.'
b'Estate developments \xe2\x80\x94 often built in combination with upscale golf courses \xe2\x80\x94 are hugely popular among wealthier homeowners in South Africa, consistently outperforming the general market, largely because they offer a combination of amenities and, as at Val de Vie, state-of-the-art security.'
b'An analysis of the sector in 2016 by Lightstone Property, a local valuations firm, identified approximately 6,500 estate developments across the country. These accounted for about 5 percent of all residential homes but more than 15 percent of the market value.'
b'Only the United States, with a population six times as large, has more of what are known there as gated communities.'
b'In a country with a long history of racial turmoil, the South Africa pattern invariably results in communities dominated by white people (only about 10 percent of South Africa\xe2\x80\x99s population), with others effectively excluded by income. Mr. van der Venter, a person of color,was a senior executive at Caltex and British American Tobacco, among other companies, before retiring and moving here.'
b'Many of the communities combine armed guards, cameras and electric fences with recreational facilities like golf courses and gyms. Some, such as Raptor\xe2\x80\x99s View, near Kruger National Park in the northeastern part of the country, include wildlife reserves.'
b'Basil Weinrich, a real estate consultant from Cape Town, said more than 40 percent of wealthy South Africans \xe2\x80\x94 defined as people earning more than about $70,000 a year\xe2\x80\x94 now either live or own property on security estates, a figure that is steadily rising.'
b'Crime dominates South African life. On average, 56 people are murdered every day, according to the most recent police statistics, making it one of the most violent countries on earth, according to the United Nations.'
b'The police recorded nearly 230,000 burglaries last year, or 625 daily. Some experts estimate that the true burglary figure is perhaps three times that high. Break-ins and car highjackings can quickly turn almost gratuitously violent, with victims beaten and raped if they do not quickly turn over valuables or open safes. Banks and high-end malls are routinely patrolled by guards holding automatic weapons.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSouth Africans consistently rank crime among their top concerns, second only to unemployment,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 said Andrew Faull, a senior researcher at the Institute for Security Studies. But, he added, \xe2\x80\x9calthough the middle class and wealthy South Africans may feel restricted by fear of crime, they are sigificantly less restricted than the poor majority.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99'
b'For people who can afford to move from \xe2\x80\x9cbubble of security to bubble of security,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, life can be \xe2\x80\x9cquite easy.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'It was almost by accident that Mr. van der Venter became converted to this way of life.'
b'For more than a decade he had owned a sprawling property in Cape Town, not perturbed by its long, dark driveway or ramshackle fencing. Then he took a job in the east coast city of Durban, moving into an estate development popular among his new colleagues.'
b'Soon afterward, he said, he began feeling \xe2\x80\x9cmentally free.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'No longer did he need to worry about locking his doors at night. His wife and children could walk around the estate after dark. He became aware of a subtle fear that had pervaded his life and was now lifting.'
b'Upon returning to Cape Town, he planned to move back into his old home. \xe2\x80\x9cBut now you realized you had this fear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe knew we were never going back.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Instead, in 2010, he and his wife purchased property at Val de Vie near Paarl. It is protected by thermal cameras, 24-hour dog patrols and an electrified, wrought-iron perimeter fence.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSafety is the main concern, of course, but you also get this incredible lifestyle,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. van der Venter said.'
b'Purchased in 2002 as a disused clay quarry, Val de Vie now has 1,500 completed homes, apartments and hotel rooms. A second phase of 1,000 units is under construction, according to the marketing director, Ryk Neethling. Vacant lots typically cost $15 to $20 per square foot, while homes go for $150 to $330 per square foot.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cMany residents who built in Phase One have already invested in Phase Two,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Neethling, a former Olympic swimming champion, on a recent tour through the development. \xe2\x80\x9cThey see the dust and construction sites and know that in a few years, it will look like this.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Properties at Val de Vie range from individualized seven-acre small holdings (\xe2\x80\x9cGentleman\xe2\x80\x99s Estates\xe2\x80\x9d) to one-bedroom apartments designed by one of South Africa\xe2\x80\x99s most prominent architects, Stefan Antoni. Most properties are family homes with gray roofs and large windows, conforming to a building code that is \xe2\x80\x9cflexible but ensures some consistency,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Neethling said.'
b'Nate Foster, a cellphone tower developer from Boulder, Colo., moved into one of these homes in January. With five bedrooms, large glass-walled living areas and a 50-foot swimming pool, it cost less than $1 million. A comparable property back home, Mr. Foster said, would have cost three to four times as much.'
b'He and his wife had been \xe2\x80\x9creally resistant\xe2\x80\x9d to lifestyle estates since relocating to Cape Town in 2015, Mr. Foster said, wanting \xe2\x80\x9cmore of a cultural experience.\xe2\x80\x9d But \xe2\x80\x9cthe realities of South Africa still exist,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cKeeping one eye open all the time wasn\xe2\x80\x99t desirable.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There\xe2\x80\x99s also the convenience factor. Mr. Foster, a triathlete, said he can train more easily at Val de Vie before going to work. A new fitness center on the estate, the Yard, has an Olympic-size swimming pool and a gym run by John McGrath, a seven-foot Irishman who used to perform in strongman competitions at Coney Island in New York.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s special about this place is the community,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. McGrath, who stays in shape by bending metal wrenches into hoops.'
b'But like Mr. Foster, the overwhelming majority of residents at Val de Vie are affluent and white. About 15 percent are foreigners, mostly from Europe, Mr. Neethling said, adding that between 10 percent and 15 percent are nonwhite.'
b'Adding more affordable housing options to the estate \xe2\x80\x94 still beyond the reach of all but the wealthiest South Africans \xe2\x80\x94 has helped broadened access without \xe2\x80\x9ccontaminating\xe2\x80\x9d prices, said Mr. van der Venter. In fact, the apartments, introduced in 2014, have been among the most successful developments at Val de Vie, with the highest average value per square foot (approximately $165) and investment returns of 100 percent in just two years.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI signed up to buy two but backed out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. van der Venter, who also owns an office block on the estate. \xe2\x80\x9cYou keep thinking this bubble is going to burst. Now I\xe2\x80\x99m kicking myself.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Even with South Africa\xe2\x80\x99s weak economy having strained property prices over the past decade, residential estates continued to perform well, said Andrew Amoils from New World Wealth.'
b'There was \xe2\x80\x9cmassive growth\xe2\x80\x9d in the sector from 2000 to 2008, but it slowed after the global financial crisis, Mr. Amoils explained. Even so, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit still consistently outperforms the general market.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In recent years, there has been a pronounced trend of developers\xe2\x80\x99 interspersing townhouses and apartments among larger houses, Mr. Amoils said. Obtaining permission to build new estates had become more difficult, particularly as concern about water use rose amid increasingly frequent droughts, and rising rates and taxes had made big homes less affordable.'
b'Still, there was the safety issue. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople get used to living like this,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Amoils said.'
b'On a recent evening, as the sun set above the polo fields, Chad Marthinussen and Chanell Classen, both 25-year-old doctors, returned to their rental apartment after work. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can come home from a crazy shift to this prettiness and just switch off,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Classen said.'
b'She grew up in Bonteheuwel, a working-class suburb outside Cape Town. She and Mr. Marthinussen are planning to marry. They both loved the estate\xe2\x80\x99s security and relaxed atmosphere, but they also shared some ambivalence.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIf we have kids, we don\xe2\x80\x99t know if we\xe2\x80\x99d want them growing up here,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Marthinussen confessed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt feels like a safe bubble. And that\xe2\x80\x99s amazing, but the real world isn\xe2\x80\x99t like this.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 It was a clash of the ages at the Masters Par-3 contest on Wednesday as 28-year-old Matt Wallace triumphed over 61-year-old Sandy Lyle during an afternoon of thrilling play that included four holes-in-one. '
b'A time-honored tradition at Augusta National, the Par-3 contest sees Masters competitors and the old guard play on a truncated course with shorter holes, with their children and spouses often serving as caddies.'
b'Along with collecting the trophy, however, comes a famous curse as no winner has gone on to win the Masters in the same year. '
b'Wallace, who kicks off his Masters bid on Thursday, shrugged off the so-called jinx, after a playoff win against two-times major winner Lyle.'
b'"It got a little bit more serious than how the nine holes went and I guess I just I wanted to win this," Wallace said. "I want to break history somewhat." '
b"Some of the game's greats, such as Gary Player and Jack Nicklaus were involved alongside current players such as Bubba Watson and Dustin Johnson, and for a crowd hungry to see a hole-in-one, the day did not disappoint."
b"Wallace landed an ace at the eighth hole, while Mark O'Meara, the 1998 Masters and British Open champion, snagged the day's first hole-in-one to wild applause, followed by 32-year-old Shane Lowry, who sunk one on the first hole."
b'Nineteen-year-old Devon Bling equaled the record for the youngest registered player ever to make a hole-in-one on the course.'
b"Last year, Nicklaus' 15-year-old grandson, who was caddying for him, sunk a hole-in-one on the ninth hole, marking a memorable family moment in an event where one is as likely to see a toddler on the course as a major winner."
b"One-year-old Azalea, named after Augusta's 13th hole, caddied for her father, 2017 Masters champion Sergio Garcia, and was an early crowd favorite as she toddled onto the green appearing completely at home on the course."

b'Seeing players on TV "it\'s like seeing a movie star," said Pam Herzwuran, who has visited the Masters for seven years. Seeing them with their families makes them feel more accessible, she said.'

b'For eight years, it seemed to Margarita Cruz that the management at the Trump Organization\xe2\x80\x99s golf club in Westchester County, N.Y., did not notice \xe2\x80\x94 or did not care \xe2\x80\x94 that the green card and Social Security card she had used to get hired were fake, purchased in Queens for about $120.'
b'Ms. Cruz, a housekeeper, said she cleaned guest rooms, offices and shops at the club. She laundered sheets and pool towels. But that all ended this month, she said.'
b'Ms. Cruz and about a dozen other employees \xe2\x80\x94 housekeepers, landscapers and a head chef \xe2\x80\x94 at the club, Trump National Golf Club, were fired Jan. 18 because they were in the country illegally, according to interviews with Ms. Cruz and the former workers\xe2\x80\x99 lawyer.'
b'The firings were first reported on Saturday by The Washington Post.'
b'The New York Times reported in December that undocumented immigrants had been employed at another club owned by the Trump Organization, the Trump National Golf Club in Bedminster, N.J., and that they were kept on the payroll for years even though management there had some knowledge of their fraudulent papers.'
b'Several workers deemed ineligible to work in the country had already been fired at the Bedminster club, according to people familiar with the matter.'
b'The employment of undocumented workers at Trump Organization properties runs counter to President Trump\xe2\x80\x99s anti-immigrant rhetoric, which he has made central to his campaign and his presidency. He is currently in a heated political battle to build a wall along the border with Mexico, which he claims would stop drugs and crime. Evidence does not support Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s thesis.'
b'There is nothing to indicate that Mr. Trump or Trump Organization executives knew about the workers\xe2\x80\x99 immigration status at either golf club. But Ms. Cruz, 44, said she believed that management at the Westchester club knew about her immigration status before January.'
b'Her papers were requested again two years ago, she said, not just when she was hired. Anibal Romero, who represents Ms. Cruz and 14 other former workers at the golf club, the majority of whom were fired Jan. 18, was stronger in his wording.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not buying that they didn\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Romero said.'
b'Neither Ms. Cruz nor the other former workers received benefits like health insurance or a pension, as other golf club employees did, Mr. Romero said. Most of his clients came into the country through Mexico, Mr. Romero said, and were originally from countries such as Mexico, Ecuador and Honduras.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThis was a two-tiered system,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe people who were legal and the people who are undocumented.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He said the workers were fired by being individually called into a room by an executive, who read their names off a list.'
b'The White House referred questions to the Trump Organization on Saturday. The Trump Organization and the Westchester golf club did not respond to requests for comment.'
b'In a statement to The Post, Eric Trump, who manages the Trump Organization with his brother Donald Jr., said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe are making a broad effort to identify any employee who has given false and fraudulent documents to unlawfully gain employment. Where identified, any individual will be terminated immediately.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ms. Cruz entered the United States in 2009 near Piedras Negras, Mexico, on the Texas border, she said, with a 12-year-old daughter and a 9-year-old son. Her parents had paid a coyote $11,000 for the trip.'
b'She said that in Mexico she earned about $40 a week working at a restaurant. She started at $11 per hour at the golf club, and then got a raise to $14 per hour. She said she did not have any savings when she was fired because she had been paying back her parents.'
b'Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s political rhetoric has been surprising, she said, because he would regularly come by the golf club before his election and give the workers $50 tips in cash.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe would come over and say hello, ask your name and how long you had worked at the club,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Cruz said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe would ask you how you liked the rug, or a picture on the wall, things like that.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'She added, \xe2\x80\x9cIf he really did hate Latinos so much, why did he come over to talk to us?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'She said some of her colleagues had worked at the golf club for 18 years.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand why he started talking like that about Latinos now and why he fired us,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'
b'Mr. Romero said he is seeking federal and state investigations of the golf club to see if it had been exploiting the undocumented workers.'

b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 Augusta National Golf Club holds a unique place in the American psyche.'
b'Slow to integrate, and with only male members until seven years ago, the private club is among the most exclusive playgrounds in the country, a haven for masters of the universe and Southern aristocracy.'
b'And then, for one week each year, the club throws opens its gates to welcome tens of thousands of \xe2\x80\x9cpatrons,\xe2\x80\x9d the world\xe2\x80\x99s top golfers and a handful of aging legends. They all line up just after sunrise and enter a world bursting with color and towering pine trees.'
b'There is an unreal quality to it. The grass underfoot doesn\xe2\x80\x99t feel like the grass in your front yard. Does that shade of pink and purple azalea exist anywhere else? How is it possible that amid all this vegetation, there are seemingly no bugs?'
b'Perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s best not to ask these questions. Answers will probably not be provided. So take in the splendor of the fans in their colorful golf shirts and sundresses reflecting off the ponds, and the magical talents of players who can skip golf balls off the water.'
b'Gaze at all those spots that had existed only on television. There\xe2\x80\x99s the Hogan Bridge over Rae\xe2\x80\x99s Creek. There\xe2\x80\x99s the patch of pine straw where Bubba Watson stood when he somehow hooked that miracle shot onto the 10th green in 2012. And look at all those green blazers. The members wear these throughout the week, in case you were wondering.'
b'Open your eyes. There is much to see.'
