b'A day after Tiger Woods won his fifth Masters title and 15th major tournament in a rousing resurrection of his career, President Trump said Monday he would give him an honor almost as exclusive as a green jacket from Augusta National Golf Club: the Presidential Medal of Freedom.'
b'Mr. Trump said on Twitter that he would bestow the award, without saying when. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause of his incredible Success & Comeback in Sports (Golf) and, more important, LIFE, I will be presenting him with the PRESIDENTIAL MEDAL OF FREEDOM!,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Trump wrote.'
b'Spoke to @TigerWoods to congratulate him on the great victory he had in yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s @TheMasters, & to inform him that because of his incredible Success & Comeback in Sports (Golf) and, more importantly, LIFE, I will be presenting him with the PRESIDENTIAL MEDAL OF FREEDOM!'
b'The Presidential Medal of Freedom is the nation\xe2\x80\x99s highest civilian honor, and is awarded to those who make outstanding contributions to national security or national interest, world peace, culture or other public or private endeavors. The medal has been awarded since 1963, and is typically given to a dozen or fewer people each year.'
b'Over the years numerous sports figures have received it, especially under the presidencies of George W. Bush, Barack Obama and Mr. Trump, all noted sports fans.'
b'Just last year Babe Ruth, Roger Staubach and Alan Page were awarded Presidential Medals of Freedom. Woods will not be the first golfer to get the award; Arnold Palmer and Jack Nicklaus were honored in consecutive years in the mid-2000s, and Charles Sifford, the first African-American to play on the PGA Tour, was honored in 2014. But it is rare for an active athlete to receive the honor.'
b'Mr. Woods has a longstanding relationship with Mr. Trump, an avid golfer. They own property near each other in Florida and have golfed together a number of times, both before and after Mr. Trump became president.'
b'In February, Mr. Trump tweeted a picture of himself with Woods and Nicklaus at the Trump National Golf Club in Jupiter, Fla. The next day, Mr. Trump wrote on Twitter, \xe2\x80\x9cTiger is back & will be winning Majors again!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Over the years Mr. Trump has repeatedly tweeted about watching Mr. Woods play golf.'
b'They are also business partners. Mr. Woods is designing the course at the Trump World Golf Club Dubai, set to open in 2019.'
b'Mr. Woods acknowledges having played golf with Mr. Trump and having eaten together, but otherwise is reluctant to speak about their relationship. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, he\xe2\x80\x99s the president of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Woods said last year. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have to respect the office.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Still, while several African-American athletes have criticized Mr. Trump or kept their distance, Mr. Woods, who is of African-American and Thai descent, has maintained his relationship with him.'
b'When asked last August after a tournament if he had any thoughts about race relations in America, Mr. Woods demurred. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I just finished 72 holes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd really hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b"Augusta, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 While the Masters is renowned for attracting the world's top players, a busy PGA tournament schedule and a field of competitors perpetually in flux means you never know who else might be in the field at Augusta National."
b'First-timer Keith Mitchell likes it that way. '
b'The 27-year-old American makes his Masters debut by virtue of securing his first PGA championship last month at the Honda Classic, claiming victory with a final, 15-foot birdie putt.'

b'"What\'s so great about our sport is the unknown, someone who you might have never heard of wins this week and then you might be able to follow him for the next ten years."'
b'While the University of Georgia graduate played the Augusta National course as a college student, he is better acquainted with life among the throngs of spectators.'

b'SAN DIEGO \xe2\x80\x94 To be the best, you have to beat the best. That\xe2\x80\x99s what Justin Rose did over the past four days to win the Farmers Insurance Open at Torrey Pines, protecting his status as the world\xe2\x80\x99s top-ranked golfer.'
b'After a shaky start Sunday that included three bogeys over his first five holes, Rose recovered to shoot a three-under-par 69 and beat his close friend Adam Scott by two shots. Rose conquered a field that featured 12 of the top 20 players in the world rankings to record his 10th PGA Tour victory, passing Nick Faldo for the most tour wins by a player from England in the modern era.'
b'Rose, 38, finished with a 72-hole total of 21-under 267, a tournament record since the South Course was redesigned in 2003 and one shot off the overall record of 22 under shared by Tiger Woods (1999) and George Burns (1987). Scott birdied the final four holes to close out a 68 and finish at 19 under. Hideki Matsuyama (67) and Talor Gooch (68) tied for fourth at 16 under.'
b'The scores were unusually low because recent rain had softened the greens, and there was very little wind coming off the Pacific Ocean.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-one under par at Torrey Pines is great golf, \xe2\x80\x9d Rose said, dedicating the win to his caddie, Mark Fulcher, who is hospitalized but doing well after having heart surgery last week. \xe2\x80\x9cThis one\xe2\x80\x99s for him.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Scott said his closing surge was \xe2\x80\x9ctoo late\xe2\x80\x9d because Rose had built a four-shot lead and birdied two of the final four holes himself. Scott also blamed himself for missing two short putts on the front nine, one from 18 inches on No. 5.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think Justin performed like the No. 1 player should today,\xe2\x80\x9d Scott said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen he was shaky, he bounced back nicely. He showed why he\xe2\x80\x99s the No. 1 player in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Rose\xe2\x80\x99s three-shot lead at the start of the day was trimmed to one after he made bogeys on Nos. 1, 4 and 5. But he played bogey-free the rest of the day while making five more birdies.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI never lost the lead, so I had to stay patient,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose said. \xe2\x80\x9cI scratched a line on my scorecard after the sixth hole and said, \xe2\x80\x98We build a round from this point on.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Scott moved to two shots behind with a tap-in birdie on the par-4 17th and needed an eagle on the 18th hole to force a playoff. But he hit his tee shot into a bunker left of the fairway and had to lay up. Both players made birdie on the final hole with short putts.'
b'In his first event of the 2019 season, Tiger Woods was never a factor. His weeklong putting woes continued Sunday on his first nine, but he rediscovered his stroke with five birdies over his final eight holes to shoot 67, finishing in a tie for 20th at 10 under. He shot all four rounds under par at Torrey Pines for the first time since 2008, and it was his first sub-70 round here since 2013, the year of his most recent of seven titles in the event.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt was nice to shake off some rust,\xe2\x80\x9d Woods said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think the whole week was good, very positive. I burned a lot of lips and made my share today, which was nice.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 After 26 Masters appearances, it is hard to catch three-time champion Phil Mickelson off guard even with a revamped fifth hole that has been the talk of Augusta National this week.'
b'Steely play may be in order at the redesigned Magnolia, where the tee has been moved back 40 yards, but Mickelson told reporters on Tuesday that he was taking it in his stride.'
b'"I think 4, 5, 6, 7 is a very difficult four-hole stretch and making it a little bit harder I think is a good thing," Mickelson said. "I always like making hard holes harder."'
b'Experience on the difficult greens is something Mickelson can rely on to help snag a Green Jacket for the fourth time, with his 100th career round at the Masters slated for Friday.'
b'"There are always little subtleties and nuances that you have to relearn," he said.'
b'"Every time I come out here and practise, I pick up a little something here or there that I did not know on how to play a certain shot from a certain position to a certain pin."'
b'If he can secure victory, it will be by defying the odds. Mickelson, who will turn 49 in June, is roughly two and a half years older than the oldest Masters champion Jack Nicklaus, who claimed his sixth victory in 1986 at the age of 46. '
b"Mickelson already has a career befitting one of the game's greats, having spent over 25 years on the PGA tour, with 195 top-10 finishes, 44 PGA championships and five major wins. "
b'That does not mean he is looking to slow down anytime soon.'
b'"There\'s no reason now with the knowledge we have in fitness, the knowledge we have in biomechanics, and the knowledge we have with nutrition and so forth, that we, at a much older age than in the past, should not be able to perform at a very high level," Mickelson said.'
b'The only major to elude him has been the U.S. Open, in which he has either tied for or placed second on six times. '
b"But after winning the Pebble Beach Pro-Am in February, it seems a career grand slam may still be in reach since this year's U.S. Open will be played on the same course."

b'Choi\xe2\x80\x99s swing was known in the United States long before he arrived at Pebble Beach. It has become a viral sensation because Choi, a South Korean native and a regular of the Japan Tour, is a cross between Chi Chi Rodriguez and Fred Astaire.'
b'Choi spins around on his follow-through as if his club is his dance partner. He pauses at the top long enough to exhale loudly, making a \xe2\x80\x9cshoo\xe2\x80\x9d sound before settling over his ball.'
b'Jordan Spieth, the three-time major winner and the 2017 champion at Pebble Beach, had seen videos of Choi and was among those eager to see him up close.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI actually was fascinated, clearly, by his swing, and the way he moves around,\xe2\x80\x9d Spieth said, adding: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just really entertaining. I think people are really excited to see him this week.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Choi, 45, did not take up the game until he was around the same age as the 25-year-old Spieth, but he has developed a showman\xe2\x80\x99s touch. The bigger the crowd, the more he appears to play to it. Upon being introduced to warm applause on the first tee, Choi bowed to the fans on three sides of the tee box before settling into his pre-shot routine.'
b'On a different tee, he took his driver head cover, decorated with studded stars, and pretended to give a back rub to Rodgers and Jerry Kelly, the other pro, by brushing it over their sweaters. Later, the other amateur, the actor Chris O\xe2\x80\x99Donnell, was teasing Rodgers about his mustache, which called to mind Burt Reynolds\xe2\x80\x99s facial hair in the 1970s film \xe2\x80\x9cSmokey and the Bandit.\xe2\x80\x9d Rodgers looked at Choi, who was standing nearby, and Choi gave him the thumbs up.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSee,\xe2\x80\x9d Rodgers said, \xe2\x80\x9cHo-sung likes it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Choi surprised Rodgers with his English abilities, after Rodgers surprised Choi by greeting him in Korean. Kelly introduced himself to Choi on the practice green with an outstretched hand and a wide grin. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will have so much fun!\xe2\x80\x9d he told him.'
b'After the round, Choi broke into a broad smile of his own and said that Kelly had been right.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think my pairing was just as good as the weather,\xe2\x80\x9d Choi said through an interpreter, referring to the abundant sunshine and negligible breeze, \xe2\x80\x9cand I learned a lot from Jerry Kelly.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Choi knows that he has a lot of catching up to do. He was training for a job in the fishing industry, he said, until he lost the tip of his right thumb in a chain saw accident at 23.'
b'Doctors reattached the tip, and Choi redirected his focus, landing a part-time job at a golf course two years later. It was there that he honed his self-taught swing.'
b'This is his first trip to the United States, he said, and he arrived with loads of enthusiasm but not a single endorsement. His black cap and turquoise sweater both featured the Pebble Beach Golf Links logo, and his caddie carried a new TaylorMade bag that seemed tailor-made for Choi, with a silhouette logo of his follow-through, complete with the kick flourish.'
b'Choi said he chose his first-round outfit purposefully. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to show my appreciation for the tournament for having me out here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m incredibly grateful, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\xe2\x80\x99m wearing the logo.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The group was led by Jonah Webster, a 16-year-old golfer from Sonora, Calif., roughly 170 miles northeast of Monterey Bay. His grandfather, Steve Lee, who walked alongside him Thursday, said Webster had trembled with excitement when he found out he would be carrying the scoring sign for Rodgers, one of his idols, and Choi, whose swing he had watched on YouTube.'
b'Lee joked that he had told his grandson to close his eyes when Choi took the club away, because his swing was not exactly something that impressionable youngsters should be trying. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s fun to pull for a guy who\xe2\x80\x99s so unique,\xe2\x80\x9d Lee said.'
b'Outside the ropes, others seemed to share Lee\xe2\x80\x99s sentiment. They cheered for every good shot and great escape orchestrated by Choi, who was four over after 10 holes but made three birdies in the final eight to salvage a one-over 72. At the end of the second round on Friday, he was tied for 128th place at four over.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI definitely felt the love from my fans,\xe2\x80\x9d Choi said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I felt like that pushed me more to focus on the back nine.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Choi\xe2\x80\x99s struggles notwithstanding, he at least impressed Rodgers. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not a sideshow,\xe2\x80\x9d the quarterback said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can play, and I think it\xe2\x80\x99s really good for golf.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'By Reuters'
b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 The fifth hole at Augusta National is certainly not the most famous, but this year it could be the hardest after being stretched so far that they had to reroute the road that ran behind the old tee. '
b'The par-four has been turned into a brute, lengthened some 40 yards to nearly 500 yards, officially 495 though the number will change slightly each day depending on where the pin is put.'
b'The tees will also likely be moved a few yards each day.'
b'"I think number five is probably going to play the toughest hole now for sure," said world number one Justin Rose.'
b'The hole, which curves gently to the left without being a dogleg, still has two deep bunkers guarding the left-hand side, encouraging players to aim more for the right side of the fairway.'
b'Rory McIlroy, perhaps the longest hitter in the field, used to worry about his ball running through the fairway and into bushes on the right, and often used a three-wood off the tee for safety instead of a driver.'
b'Now he can swing away without fear.'
b'"I think (hole) five has been a very good change in terms (that) it puts driver back in a lot of guys\' hands that wouldn\'t necessarily hit driver on that hole," said the Northern Irishman.'
b'"And you need to hit driver; because I came here last Wednesday, played in the morning, it was a little cold, a little damp and I hit four-iron into the green.'
b'"If you hit three-wood and you\'re 30 yards back of (where driver would stop) you\'re on the upslope and you can hardly see the green."'
b'Tiger Woods succinctly summed up the new hole.'
b'"It\'s just long," he said on Wednesday. "The bunkers are still deep. I think they are unplayable to get the ball to the green."'
b'The hole was parred more than two-thirds of the time in 2018, when it yielded only 26 birdies all week, along with 64 bogeys and four double-bogeys. '
b'It was the sixth hardest of the week.'
b'It is likely to give up even fewer birdies this week, but a lot more bogeys.'
b'"I always like making hard holes harder," said three-times Masters champion Phil Mickelson.'
b'For the shorter hitters, the game plan has not really changed.'
b'"The tee shot is pretty much the same shot," said British Open champion Francesco Molinari, who is not a particularly long hitter.'
b'"The second shot, obviously is longer, considerably longer. Yesterday it was playing into the wind, and probably with the old tee we would have been hitting seven-iron in, and we were hitting four-iron in yesterday. '
b'"I think that\'s going to be around a three-club difference."'

b'By Michael Arkush'
b'On Sunday, April 15, 1979, Ed Sneed was about to win the Masters. He was leading by three shots with three holes to go. Then, suddenly, the game became as cruel as it can possibly be.'
b'Sneed, who had started the day ahead by five, bogeyed 16, 17 and 18 \xe2\x80\x94 his par putt on 18 hung on the lip \xe2\x80\x94 then lost on the second hole of a sudden-death playoff to Fuzzy Zoeller. Sneed, who is now 74 and finished his career with four tour victories, never came close again to winning a major. He left in 1985 to go into the investment business.'
b'In a recent interview, Sneed reflected on that time. The conversation has been edited and condensed.'
b'How often does someone bring up 1979?'
b'For the first five years, there would be articles in magazines the week or month before the Masters. Every year, since that Masters, there\xe2\x80\x99s been at least one or two guys who have called me. This year, there seems to be more focus on it, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve had quite a few calls because it\xe2\x80\x99s the 40th anniversary.'
b'How did losing that tournament affect your life?'
b'I probably would have played the tour longer. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it made me basically a different person. I\xe2\x80\x99ve often said, in my reaction, nobody died. I had a chance to win a big golf tournament, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t. It did have an impact, but at the same time, I never felt like it destroyed me in any way.'
b'Watching the final round recently, it looked as if there were not any shots on the last three holes you\xe2\x80\x99d want to take back except the approach on 18, which missed the green. Is that right?'
b'That\xe2\x80\x99s right. I always heard, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you hit to the right at 16.\xe2\x80\x9d I didn\xe2\x80\x99t hit to the right at 16. I hit a draw. Why the ball didn\xe2\x80\x99t bounce to the left, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. At 17, [where his approach finished a few yards over the green] I discovered the next year I had the wrong yardage by three or four yards.'
b'You thought the putt on 18 was in, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you?'
b'I thought the putt would just barely move to the right. So, I hit the ball at the left side of the cup. And when it was about two feet away, it looked like it had moved a little bit toward the hole. I thought it was going right in, and then it hung on the left. It looked like a quarter of the ball was hanging over the hole.'
b'The next year, I was playing a practice round on Wednesday afternoon after the Par 3 tournament. I was by myself. I started at 10. At the 11th green, Hord Hardin, the [acting] tournament chairman, told me, \xe2\x80\x9cEd, that was tough luck last year, but we feel that you waited about 12 seconds to tap it in, and under the rules you only had 10, and if it had fallen in, we would have been in a dilemma, and we probably would have had to penalize you a stroke.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'You realized pretty soon that how you\xe2\x80\x99d respond to the loss would be a test.'
b'I ended up going to the Tallahassee Open the next week, and one of the press guys sat down next to me, and this is something I\xe2\x80\x99ve always held on to. When I got back to the house in Augusta that night, my girls at the time were 4\xc2\xbd years old and barely 2. My 4\xc2\xbd-year-old had been watching television. When I walked in, she came running up to me and said, \xe2\x80\x9cDaddy, you won, you won.\xe2\x80\x9d I realized that it didn\xe2\x80\x99t make any difference to them. I was still their dad.'
b'When people ask me, \xe2\x80\x9cDo I think about it?\xe2\x80\x9d I sure do. Not as much now as I used to. I don\xe2\x80\x99t dwell on it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never felt like, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy me?\xe2\x80\x9d You look at things that go on around the world, and other people\xe2\x80\x99s circumstances. What did I do? I missed a putt to win the Masters. If that\xe2\x80\x99s the worst thing that happened to me, even in just golf, so be it.'

b"AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 Thirty-three years since Jack Nicklaus won the Masters at age 46, he remains the oldest claimant of the Green Jacket and perhaps only Phil Mickelson among this year's competitors is a realistic chance to break the Golden Bear's mark."
b'But 61-year-old Bernhard Langer thinks that sooner or later a grey-haired older player will win a major, perhaps even the Masters.'
b'Nicklaus showed what is possible not just by winning here in 1986 but by going close 12 years later in what would have been a victory of historic proportions.'
b"He was in contention until the final few holes before eventually finishing equal sixth, four shots back of winner Mark O'Meara."
b'But for all the talk of the value of experience at Augusta National, it has been an exception rather than the rule for players over 50 to contend for the Green Jacket.'
b'Spaniard Miguel Jimenez finished fourth in 2014, three months after his 50th birthday, while the performance of Langer that same year was also impressive as he finished equal eighth at age 56.'
b'Langer, never a particularly long hitter even in his prime, says the length of Augusta National eventually catches up with him over 72 holes.'
b'He thinks a longer hitter is more likely to crack the code, someone who can still almost match distance with the young bucks from the tee.'
b'"It\'s a lot harder for me now than 20-30 years ago," 1985 and 1993 champion Langer told Reuters.'
b'"The course has gotten longer. The last few years I\'m wearing out my three and four-iron and two-hybrid into these par-fours, whereas these young guys are hitting eight-irons. '
b'"It\'s just hard to compete when you have that disadvantage."'
b'Langer gets a sobering baptism these days right off the bat at the par-four first, which now stretches to 445-yards. '
b'When the hole plays into a cool north-westerly headwind, his drive invariably longs on the upslope, leaving him so far from the green that he has a blind second shot.'
b'"I can\'t even see the flag (sometimes)," he said.'
b'Not even meticulous course management and a consistent putting stroke can make up for the 50 yards or more head start that he is giving younger players off the tee.'
b'Even if his chances of winning are gone, Langer reckons someone of the ilk of 1992 champion Fred Couples or 2000 winner Vijay Singh stands a better chance of winning in his 50s.'
b'Though he is not yet 50, Mickelson at age 48 and already a winner on the PGA Tour at Pebble Beach this year is a definite threat.'
b"This week's field will include seven players aged 50 or older."
b'"Someone who is 20 yards longer than me, I think they still have a chance," Langer said.'

b'LOS ANGELES \xe2\x80\x94 Matt Kuchar had heard enough. Battered by criticism that he had essentially stiffed a caddie who helped him win a tournament last November in Mexico, he took action to make amends on Friday, issuing a statement in which he apologized for his initial actions and also said he had now paid the caddie a good deal more money.'
b'In the 15-line statement, which was released while he was playing the rain-delayed first and second rounds of the Genesis Open in Los Angeles, Kuchar said he had now given the caddie, David Giral Ortiz, a much bigger sum than the $5,000 he originally paid him after capturing the first-place prize of $1.296 million at the Mayakoba Golf Classic.'
b'In issuing the statement, Kuchar clearly hoped to put an end to the bad tidings that had sullied his image and cast him as a tone-deaf American with a let-them-eat-cake attitude.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cGolf is a game where we call penalties on ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d Kuchar said in the statement. \xe2\x80\x9cI should have done that long ago and not let this situation escalate.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The statement did not specify how much more Kuchar had sent Ortiz\xe2\x80\x99s way, but it appears an additional $45,000 was paid out.'
b'Ortiz had been asking for a total of $50,000, according to Marc Graubart, a Houston businessman working on the caddie\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, and Kuchar, in his statement, said Ortiz had now been paid what he was seeking.'
b'Kuchar\xe2\x80\x99s apology came at the end of a trying week for him, and after a challenging first round on Friday at the Riviera Country Club, where he heard the usual genial chants of \xe2\x80\x9cKooooch\xe2\x80\x9d from some of his fans but also a number of barbs from others about his perceived parsimony.'
b'Kuchar, 40, has earned more than $45 million as a professional golfer, but his problems began when he won a tournament in Playa del Carmen, Mexico, that he had basically entered on a whim.'
b'In a typical PGA Tour arrangement, a caddie receives a flat rate of $1,500 for a tournament, which helps cover expenses, and a bonus worth 10 percent of the golfer\xe2\x80\x99s winnings. For Ortiz, a local caddie who is also known as El Tucan, that would have amounted to about $130,000.'
b'Instead, Ortiz told Michael Bamberger of Golf.com, he and Kuchar had agreed on a $3,000 payment plus an unspecified amount of whatever money Kuchar won at the tournament.'
b'Kuchar, meanwhile, said he had promised Ortiz $1,000 if he missed the cut, $2,000 if he made the cut, $3,000 for a top-20 finish and $4,000 for a top-10 finish, with no specifications for how much more he would pay Ortiz if he finished first.'
b'Which Kuchar proceeded to do, ending a four-year winless drought. At that point, Kuchar gave Ortiz $5,000 \xe2\x80\x94 the $4,000 he said they had agreed upon for the top-10 finish and a $1,000 bonus. Kuchar confirmed that total to Golf.com and added, \xe2\x80\x9cFor a guy who makes $200 a day, a $5,000 week is a really big week.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But the tone and content of that comment were quickly pounced on, particularly by people from outside the insular world of golf. And some golf fans weren\xe2\x80\x99t too pleased by Kuchar\xe2\x80\x99s attitude, either. As Kuchar walked down the first fairway on Friday morning, one fan stopped walking and shouted in Kuchar\xe2\x80\x99s direction. \xe2\x80\x9cFrugal, just frugal,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 the fan yelled. \xe2\x80\x9cI pay my caddie more.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The fan, Brent Weiss, who described himself as a kindergarten teacher, said Kuchar had been his favorite player. But not anymore.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you tip a caddie $1,000 when you\xe2\x80\x99ve made $1.3 million?\xe2\x80\x9d Weiss said. \xe2\x80\x9cTo me, I think that\xe2\x80\x99s called discrimination.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Weiss added, \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t even look at the guy.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Not surprisingly, Kuchar also found himself the target of increasing indignation on social media.'
b'In his statement on Friday, Kuchar, who is considered one of the more affable players on the tour, said he viewed his initial comments in defense of the $5,000 payment as \xe2\x80\x9cout of touch and insensitive.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey made it seem like I was marginalizing David Ortiz and his financial situation, which was not my intention,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 the statement added. \xe2\x80\x9cI read them again and cringed. That is not who I am and not what I want to represent.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Kuchar declined to comment as he walked off the course on Friday. When play was halted for the day, he still had five holes to complete in the second round.'
b'A nine-time Tour winner, Kuchar serves as a brand spokesman for several companies, including Skechers shoes, Bridgestone Golf and Royal Bank of Canada. Before turning professional in 2000, Kuchar, who had starred at Georgia Tech, worked for a year as an investment banker.'
b'Ortiz, a father of two, lives in a rented house and takes the bus to his job at the golf course. And the fact that he was paid only $5,000 by Kuchar might not have become as widely known as it did were it not for the efforts of Graubart, who regularly employs Ortiz during golf vacations in Mexico.'
b'In a telephone interview on Thursday, Graubart said he happened to be with Ortiz in Mexico last month when questions about how much Kuchar had paid him surfaced. They saw Kuchar on television during a tournament in Hawaii, telling reporters that he had paid Ortiz more than $3,000 for his services but less than 10 percent of his total earnings.'
b'Graubart said he then quizzed Ortiz about the details of his unwritten agreement with Kuchar. At which point, Graubart said, he explained the typical pay scale to Ortiz, who seemed unaware that he could have earned more money.'
b'Graubart said he and a friend helped Ortiz write emails to Kuchar\xe2\x80\x99s agent, Mark Steinberg, requesting more compensation. In a Jan. 23 email, which The New York Times obtained, Ortiz wrote that he had asked Kuchar for a percentage of his first-place check if he were to win. The existence of the emails was first reported by Golf.com.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cMatt said \xe2\x80\x98sure\xe2\x80\x99 with a smile,\xe2\x80\x9d Ortiz wrote in the email, \xe2\x80\x9cnot believing that he would actually win the tournament.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'A few days later, Graubart said, Ortiz received an offer of $20,000 from a tournament director who appeared to be acting on behalf of Kuchar\xe2\x80\x99s agent. But Graubart said Ortiz had requested $50,000, and the caddie then wrote an email asking if he could deal directly with Steinberg, who replied: \xe2\x80\x9cI am out of the country. What Matt has offered is fair.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Some golf fans clearly disagreed. A GoFundMe account had been set up on Ortiz\xe2\x80\x99s behalf and late on Thursday night, the total had reached $2,226.'
b'But Kuchar had a significant change of heart, and now Ortiz has a lot more than that in his bank account.'

b'(Reuters) - Ko Jin-young joined the long list of South Korean LPGA major champions on Sunday with a three-shot victory over compatriot Lee Mi-hyang at the ANA Inspiration in Rancho Mirage, California.'
b"Ko, last year's Rookie of the Year, overcame a late wobble in the final round to became the fifth Korean to win the event after Grace Park (2004), Yoo Sun-young (2012), Park In-bee (2013) and Ryu So-yeon (2017)."
b'She is the 15th woman from South Korea to win a major, the most of any country other than the United States, and the victory is projected to see her top the world rankings.'
b'"I had a great round and a great week. I felt nervous today but I just tried to keep myself calm," said Ko.'
b'After her lead had been reduced to one shot with three holes to play following two quick bogeys Ko responded like a true champion, a perfectly-judged 10-foot birdie putt at the 16th proving the decisive blow at Mission Hills .'
b'She clinched in style with another birdie at the last for a two-under-par 70, bursting into tears when the putt fell.'
b'"My caddie told me on the 18th that I had a two-shot lead and to not worry, just enjoy it," Ko said.'
b'"So many Korean players had a great tournament, and it\'s a real honor for me to win."'
b'Ko finished at 10-under 278, while Lee carded 70 for second place on seven-under, a shot ahead of American Lexi Thompson (67).'
b'Kim In-kyung, who started the day in second place, fell out of contention with a double-bogey at the 11th, where her ball got stuck up a tree, forcing her to take a penalty stroke.'
b'She shot 74 and tied for fourth with Spaniard Carlota Ciganda, five shots behind Ko.'
b"The 23-year-old Ko said she had been dreaming of winning the event for five years and had always wanted to make the traditional winner's jump into Poppie's Pond."
b'"I always think about when I can go in that pond," she said. "That\'s today."'
b'Despite the distractions and pressure of leading a major on a Sunday, Ko said she got over the line by trusting her swing.'
b'"I just focused on my swing, on the putting, that\'s why I win this week," she said.'
b'"I won. I can believe it now."'

b'AUSTIN, Tex. \xe2\x80\x94 Rory McIlroy never trailed in his first three matches at Austin Country Club, advancing to the round of 16 at the World Golf Championships match-play event without having to play the 17th or 18th holes. He was looking to extend his front-running ways in an elimination match against Tiger Woods, whose superlative career has been a sucker pin that McIlroy recently decided to stop taking dogged aim at.'
b'McIlroy, 29, has 15 PGA Tour titles, including four majors, which makes him the leader of the men\xe2\x80\x99s 20-something set but far behind a pre-30-year-old Woods\xe2\x80\x99s pace of 46 PGA Tour titles, including 10 majors.'
b'McIlroy came into this week as perhaps the hottest player in the men\xe2\x80\x99s game, with six top-six finishes in his first six starts in 2019, including a victory at the Players Championship. But unlike Woods, whose stated goal when he was McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s age was to win every time he played, McIlroy spoke earlier in the week of the likelihood that he would fail since he wins less than 10 percent of his starts. With odds like that, McIlroy has learned to measure success in other ways, like framing the struggles as growth opportunities and accentuating the positives.'
b'McIlroy had never faced Woods, 43, in match play, and he clearly had no idea how quickly the conditions could change from warm to chilly. There was the rain and wind that blew in on the back nine, which was forecast. But McIlroy ought to also have been prepared to face an opponent, in Woods, who was a dispassionate adversary intent on making putts, not conversation.'
b'McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s easy familiarity is with Woods, the friend and mentor. He sometimes practices with Woods, his South Florida neighbor, and he and his family regularly dine at the restaurant that Woods owns. McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s last two major victories came in 2014, the year of Woods\xe2\x80\x99s worsening back problems, which led to four operations and two essentially lost seasons.'
b'So as McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s stature was growing, he got only glimpses of Woods, the foe and mentalist whose competitive fire often reduced his would-be rivals to ash.'
b'On Saturday, a sizable congregation of early-rising Woods worshipers watched a match of dueling personalities \xe2\x80\x94 Woods\xe2\x80\x99s intensity versus McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s inclusivity. In the end, the edge belonged to Woods, whose 2-and-1 victory slowed McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s momentum as he rolls into Augusta for the Masters in two weeks.'
b'In the afternoon quarterfinals, Lucas Bjerregaard sent Woods packing with a 1-up defeat, which Woods described as a snag in his pre-Masters preparation. \xe2\x80\x9cI was really hoping to play Sunday,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, referring to the semifinals and final.'
b'Woods\xe2\x80\x99s match against McIlroy, which ended when Woods drained a 15-foot par putt to halve the par-3 17th hole, began to turn on the par-4 fifth when McIlroy had an 8-foot birdie putt to go 1-up. He backed away from the attempt and waited for more than a minute while the players on an adjacent fairway hit their shots. The pause gave McIlroy plenty of time to think about the putt, which he then missed.'
b'Funnily enough, McIlroy might have been one of the few people at the hole who was aware that there were others in action. This was Woods\xe2\x80\x99s first appearance as a professional in Austin, and fans flocked to watch him play as if he may never again come their way.'
b'McIlroy tried to draw Woods into casual conversation early in the match only to have his attempts peter out like short putts. They did not talk during a lengthy delay on the fifth tee while they waited for the fairway to clear. McIlroy idly bounced a ball several times on the face of his driver. Woods took his ball, placed it between his right index and middle fingers and flipped it in the air and caught it between the same two fingers, a sequence he repeated several times.'
b'McIlroy missed a 3-footer to lose the 10th hole, extending Woods\xe2\x80\x99s lead to 3-up. But as the weather turned, so, too, did the momentum. As the wind began to whip and a cold rain started to fall, McIlroy won the 12th and 13th holes with his first birdies of the day. The match looked as if it might be getting away from Woods when he hit his drive at the par-4 15th way left, where water lurked. But his ball bounced off someone or something and came to rest in grass trampled down by fans. After a great third shot, Woods sank a nervy 8-footer to match McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s par.'
b'If Woods\xe2\x80\x99s putter hurt McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s comeback hopes, the final blow was self-inflicted. On the par-5 16th, McIlroy\xe2\x80\x99s drive traveled 395 yards. He was in the fairway, 173 yards from the pin, and somehow made what amounted to a 7 on the hole. With Woods on the green in three, McIlroy produced his worst swing of the day. The ball landed in the rough near the lip of a greenside bunker, and he pitched his next shot over the green as fans in the vicinity scattered.'
b'The ball came to rest against a wooden bulkhead, and McIlroy took an unplayable lie, leading to Woods winning the hole without having to hit a putt. McIlroy was muttering to himself as he walked to the 17th tee, but after Woods ended the match on the 17th green, McIlroy had nothing to say.'
b'He hopped into a cart and left the grounds without talking to reporters. It was a rare fit of pique from McIlroy, who has worked hard over the past year to moderate his emotions no matter what score he cards.'
b'Had McIlroy learned from his loss to Woods that constraint can feed a competitor\xe2\x80\x99s fire? It would appear not. In an Instagram post later in the day, McIlroy apologized for his abrupt departure and added that he would \xe2\x80\x9clearn and move on.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'Golf is all about how you react when you get a bad bounce.'
b'By Thomas L. Friedman'
b'Opinion Columnist'
b'Although my day job is writing the foreign affairs column for The New York Times \xe2\x80\x94 more Persian Gulf than fairway golf \xe2\x80\x94 thinking about golf and playing as often as I can is my all-consuming hobby. So like millions of others, I was awed by Tiger Woods\xe2\x80\x99s comeback for the ages by his winning the Masters at 43 years old. What can be learned from it?'
b'It\xe2\x80\x99s hard for nongolfers to appreciate the scope of Tiger\xe2\x80\x99s physical and psychological achievement, after he went through four back surgeries and the global tabloid exposure of his industrial-scale marital cheating.'
b'If I think of the news I normally cover, it would be as if Bill Clinton came back and defeated Donald Trump for president in 2020. Or, in technology, it\xe2\x80\x99s the equivalent of Steve Jobs founding Apple, losing Apple and then coming back and winning all four \xe2\x80\x9ctechnology majors\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x94 the Apple desktop, laptop, iPhone and iPad \xe2\x80\x94 with a reborn Apple.'
b'The biggest takeaway for me is the reminder of the truism that golf is the sport most like life, because it is played on an uneven surface and everything is on you. So good and bad bounces \xe2\x80\x94 and self-inflicted mistakes \xe2\x80\x94 are built into the game. And so much of success in golf, as in life, is about how you react to those good and bad bounces. Do you quit? Do you throw your club? Do you cheat? Do you whine? Do you blame your caddie?'
b'Or do you say what the greatest golfers say when a bounce turns against them or their ball ends in a divot in the middle of the fairway and only a great shot will get them back into the hole? They all say to their caddie the same two words: \xe2\x80\x9cWatch this.\xe2\x80\x9d And then they pull off a remarkable shot that winds through the trees, over the hill and past the sand trap, avoids the pond on the left and lands right in the middle of the putting green \xe2\x80\x94 which is exactly the shot Tiger hit on the 11th hole out of the trees at Augusta National on Sunday.'
b'To do that under pressure is stunning, but it is not just luck or even pure physical attributes. It is about practice \xe2\x80\x94 hours and hours and hours. Gary Player liked to say, \xe2\x80\x9cThe more I practice the luckier I get.\xe2\x80\x9d And that is where, for me, the meaning of Tiger\xe2\x80\x99s comeback begins: his willingness to commit to endless hours of physical rehabilitation and then endless hours of practice. How many of us have that iron will? But the physical part is the least of it.'
b'Last year I wrote the foreword to a golf instruction book by my teacher, in which I argued that what makes golf so difficult \xe2\x80\x94 but also so satisfying when you get it right \xe2\x80\x94 is that you have to combine four things: physics, geometry, geography and psychology. Tiger\xe2\x80\x99s genius on Sunday, and so often throughout his career, is his ability to master all four better than anyone else.'
b'How so? Well, every golf swing always starts with a geography quiz: How far away is that target and how is it set in the landscape? Is it elevated? Is it on a ledge? Is it in a bowl? Is it by an ocean cliff, a stream or a pond?'
b'Then comes the geometry lesson: At what angle and speed do I have to launch this little white ball to match, or take advantage of, this geography and get as close as possible to the hole, taking into account the wind and temperature and landscape as well?'
b'Then comes the physics lesson: How do I move my arms and legs and shift my weight \xe2\x80\x94 and at what speed, torque, size of backswing, ball position and swing finish \xe2\x80\x94 to ensure that my little white ball solves this geography-geometry riddle and lands as close as possible to my target?'
b'And, finally, what emotional and psychological mind-set do I need to increase the odds that my body and arms will move into the ideal launch positions \xe2\x80\x94 to achieve the geography-geometry solution that I\xe2\x80\x99ve designed in my head. As any golfer will tell you, there is no greater buzz in the world than solving that puzzle with your body and watching your golf ball soar toward its intended target with the perfect geometry against the backdrop of some stunning geography.'
b'Tiger did that several times Sunday, and you could feel the buzz, and none more intensely than on Augusta National\xe2\x80\x99s perilous 16th hole, where he launched his ball on the perfect arc over the water, softly curbing right to left with the terrain and then landing in the precise two-foot-diameter circle so that it would then roll 20 feet down the slope and stop 15 inches below the hole. Geography, geometry, physics and psychology all working in perfect unison.'
b'You cannot overestimate the psychological aspect of that shot. Golf is such a head game, and if you are distracted by something, you\xe2\x80\x99ll never put the geography, geometry and physics together at the level needed to win in professional golf. That\xe2\x80\x99s why Tiger\xe2\x80\x99s game deteriorated so far after his infidelities had been broadcast all over the world in 2009, and even before his back gave out. You could actually see it when Tiger walked through a golf gallery back then. His eye never wanted to meet those of his fans, because he knew that they knew that he knew that they knew that he\xe2\x80\x99d been a first-class jerk.'
b'What was in his head translated into his hands, and it translated into his scores. For the better part of a decade, he could not win a major until his back was healed and he got the monkey of his own misdeeds off his back \xe2\x80\x94 by becoming a good father and a better person to his fans and his fellow golfers. You could see him looking everyone in the eye in the last couple of years, and it finally unlocked his fan base. It gave them permission to root for him again, full-throated, despite all the ways he\xe2\x80\x99d disappointed them. And that clearly unlocked his mind, and I am sure his body, too, so he could swing freely again.'
b'And that leads to another way that golf is so much like life. Each and every round is a journey, and, like all of life\xe2\x80\x99s journeys, it\xe2\x80\x99s never a straight line. It\xe2\x80\x99s always full of crazy bounces, self-inflicted mistakes and unexpected detours, and therefore always a journey of discovery about yourself and your playing partners. And, if you love the game, it\xe2\x80\x99s an everlasting journey in search of self-improvement \xe2\x80\x94 always trying to get your geography, geometry, physics and psychology in perfect alignment.'
b'And when you see it done at the highest level, on the toughest terrain, under the most intense spotlight by someone who had it, lost it and then got it back, you can only say: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a privilege! I saw Tiger make his comeback and win the Masters at age 43. What a crazy, wonderful, amazing journey!\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 As Patrick Reed surveyed his second shot, he seemed to have a dozen worries on his mind.'
b'Which way was the wind blowing? Where on the green should he land his ball? Would the fans in his peripheral vision stop moving?'
b'In a sport where the ball is still and the mind is active, and where distractions from unruly fans, bad bounces or sudden gusts of wind abound, focus can be difficult to maintain. But during the first round of the 2018 Tour Championship in Atlanta, it became nearly impossible. Reed took one last look at his target and what he saw nearly made his head go haywire.'
b'Why was his estranged father standing behind the green?'
b'No one on the PGA Tour these days has his powers of concentration tested as much as Reed, whose sometimes brash personality generates intense reaction among his peers and the public alike. For every person like the two-time major winner Curtis Strange, who said that he admires Reed\xe2\x80\x99s self-belief, there are dozens who will never forgive him for proclaiming himself \xe2\x80\x9ca top-five player in the world\xe2\x80\x9d when he was 23 years old and ranked No. 20.'
b'Yet easily the worst distraction that Reed faces any week, but especially this one, when he will defend the Masters title he won last year, is the possibility that at any moment he will look up and come face-to-face with the most painful chapter of his life.'
b'Reed\xe2\x80\x99s parents live six miles from Augusta National Golf Club, in a two-story, Southern-style Colonial replete with a bedroom shrine to their first child and only son, who hasn\xe2\x80\x99t stepped foot in the house since 2012. This week should be a joyous homecoming for Reed, who led Augusta State (now Augusta University) to back-to-back national championships and will preside over Tuesday\xe2\x80\x99s legends-laden Champions dinner. But instead it has all the makings of a nightmare, with his acrimonious relationship with his family threatening to become as much a part of this year\xe2\x80\x99s Masters narrative as his attempt to become the first golfer since Tiger Woods in 2002 to successfully defend his title.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t at all be surprised if they show up,\xe2\x80\x9d Reed said.'
b'Reed, 28, has steadfastly declined to speak publicly about the reasons for the family schism. In a Sports Illustrated story in 2015, Reed\xe2\x80\x99s mother insinuated that the rift resulted from Reed\xe2\x80\x99s marriage, at age 22, to the former Justine Karain, against the advice of his parents who worried that he was too young.'
b'Reed\xe2\x80\x99s parents were not invited to last year\xe2\x80\x99s Masters, when their son held off a challenge from Rickie Fowler and Jordan Spieth down the stretch, just as they were not invited to his 2012 wedding. They have not met either of his two young children. To attend this week\xe2\x80\x99s tournament, they will have to enter the grounds with badges they did not obtain from him.'
b'Last year the drama was confined to the course, but in the year since, Reed said he knew of two occasions in which his father and younger sister, Hannah, followed him at golf tournaments. b'Danny Coulson, a former deputy assistant director of the F.B.I. who directs the PGA Tour\xe2\x80\x99s security, saw Reed\xe2\x80\x99s father at the first of the two events where Reed also noticed him, last September\xe2\x80\x99s Tour Championship at Atlanta\xe2\x80\x99s East Lake Golf Club. Coulson said he had cordially explained to Bill Reed what he and his staff have expressed over the years to Patrick: If Reed\xe2\x80\x99s father wishes to watch golf, he is free to do so unless he does or says anything that would warrant his ejection.'
b'Reed had other guests at the Tour Championship. He is extremely close to the Karains. b'Reed was wild off the tee that day, hitting only six fairways, but later said his problems were strictly self-inflicted. If he had noticed his father and sister on the front nine, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI would have called for security to throw them out.\xe2\x80\x9d But those were his emotions boiling over after a round so frustrating that he went straight to the range after signing his scorecard and hit dozens of balls.'
b'The week after the Tour Championship, Reed\xe2\x80\x99s father and sister made the 4,300-mile trek to Paris for the Ryder Cup, where they followed Reed during his Sunday singles victory. b'Well, maybe a little. b'AUGUSTA, Ga. \xe2\x80\x94 The methodical, ever calculating Bryson DeChambeau, who last week put himself through a 14-hour practice session to decipher the effect of what he called the \xe2\x80\x9cspin loft curve\xe2\x80\x9d of his wedges, was asked late Thursday to assess the final nine holes of his first round at the 2019 Masters.'

b'An undocumented immigrant who worked at one of President Trump\xe2\x80\x99s golf clubs in New Jersey for years and recently spoke out about her experience will attend his State of the Union address after being invited by her Democratic congresswoman, the woman\xe2\x80\x99s lawyer and the congresswoman\xe2\x80\x99s office said Wednesday.'
b'The undocumented immigrant, Victorina Morales, had worked as a housekeeper at the Trump National Golf Club in Bedminster, N.J., since 2013, and spoke to The New York Times as part of an article published last month. The report revealed that the president\xe2\x80\x99s company \xe2\x80\x94 the Trump Organization \xe2\x80\x94 was, for years, employing people at the golf club who are in the country illegally.'
b'In a brief interview on Wednesday, Ms. Morales said that on the advice of her lawyer, she stopped going to work on Dec. 4, two days before the Times article was published. Still, the lawyer, Anibal Romero, said that neither he nor his client had received notice that she had been fired. He said Ms. Morales\xe2\x80\x99s employment status with the golf club was not entirely clear.'
b'Since leaving her job, Ms. Morales, who is from Guatemala, has campaigned for rights and fair treatment for undocumented immigrants. On Wednesday, she said she was pleased to have been invited to the State of the Union, that she has accepted the invitation and that she was \xe2\x80\x9cvery proud because I am going to raise my voice for all of us immigrants.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not scared to show my face,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, speaking in Spanish. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not speaking for me, I\xe2\x80\x99m speaking on behalf of millions of undocumented immigrants who live in the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The congresswoman, Bonnie Watson Coleman, represents the New Jersey district in which Ms. Morales lives. The news that Ms. Morales had been invited to attend Tuesday\xe2\x80\x99s State of the Union address as Ms. Watson Coleman\xe2\x80\x99s guest was reported by The Washington Post on Wednesday.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cImmigrants by and large are hardworking, trustworthy and skilled people who simply want to work and build better lives here,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Watson Coleman said in a statement. \xe2\x80\x9cFor years these kinds of people were loyal and dedicated enough to be Trump Organization employees. b'The White House did not immediately respond to a request for comment on Wednesday evening.'
b'Mr. Trump has made border security and protecting jobs for Americans cornerstones of his presidency, and his insistence on building a border wall led to the recent partial government shutdown. He has labeled illegal immigration \xe2\x80\x9ca major, major problem,\xe2\x80\x9d and pledged to build a border wall while his administration has carried out workplace raids and payroll audits.'
b'The Times article, published Dec. 6, said undocumented immigrants had been employed for years at the Trump National Golf Club as housekeepers, landscapers and kitchen staff. It also said they were kept on the payroll even though management was aware they had used phony documents to secure employment, as is common among undocumented immigrants.'
b'Ms. Morales previously told The Times that a manager at Bedminster had helped her secure a new fake Social Security number and legal permanent-residency card after telling her that those on file had expired.'
b'Since the article was published, about a dozen workers deemed ineligible to work in the United States because they lacked legal immigration status have been terminated at the Bedminster club, according to people familiar with the matter.'
b'Another dozen were fired at the Trump National Golf Club in Westchester County, N.Y., this month, a development first reported by The Washington Post.'
b'The Trump Organization also said late Tuesday that it was putting in place a system to weed out undocumented immigrants who try to get jobs at its properties.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI must say, for me personally, this whole thing is truly heartbreaking,\xe2\x80\x9d Eric Trump, an executive vice president with the Trump Organization, said in the statement on Tuesday. \xe2\x80\x9cOur employees are like family, but when presented with fake documents, an employer has little choice.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He said that hiring undocumented immigrants was not a problem unique to the Trump Organization, and that it \xe2\x80\x9cdemonstrates that our immigration system is severely broken and needs to be fixed immediately.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'With a smile, Woods said: \xe2\x80\x9cAccidents happen, and you move on. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had galleries run over me. When you play in front of a lot of people, things happen. But it\xe2\x80\x99s all good.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Woods insisted he was not injured in the accident, which looked a little like a base runner trying to take out an infielder at second on a double-play attempt.'
b'When pressed on whether he was physically sound for the Masters\xe2\x80\x99 weekend rounds, Woods grinned and answered: \xe2\x80\x9cYeah, other than having four knee surgeries and four back surgeries, I\xe2\x80\x99m fine. Good to go.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'On a day when most of the top scores were produced by golfers teeing off in the morning, with the sun out, Woods\xe2\x80\x99s ascent up the leaderboard as an evening rainstorm drenched the golf course was something of a surprise.'
b'At one point, it seemed the poor weather could undermine Woods, as play was suspended just after he hit his tee shot to the menacing 12th green, about 5 feet behind the hole.'
b'When play resumed 29 minutes later, Woods, who admitted he grew stiff during the delay, missed the birdie putt. Then his short birdie putt on the 13th hole stopped an inch in front of the hole\xe2\x80\x99s center. At the time, Woods was stuck at four under for the tournament.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI had a few chances go the wrong way, but I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t too bummed about it because I also hit a lot of really good shots,\xe2\x80\x9d Woods said later. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I was still confident.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Indeed, Woods was at his scrambling best, reaching 16 of 18 greens in regulation despite missing half of the fairways on the 14 par-4 and par-5 holes.'
b'He missed a 7-foot birdie putt at the 17th hole and a 15-footer at the final hole. Converting either of them would have put him in a tie for the lead.'
b'Afterward, Woods could not resist stressing that he has now been a contender in three consecutive major golf championships, including last year\xe2\x80\x99s British Open and P.G.A. Championship.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI felt very good out there doing what I was doing, and so now this is three straight majors that I\xe2\x80\x99ve been in the mix,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cSo it\xe2\x80\x99s good stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Most of the attention early Friday was on Molinari, which was unusual because he is rarely included in the featured groupings of top golfers selected by tournament officials. And even as Molinari was shooting a bogey-free 67, he toured Augusta National largely unrecognized.'
b'But Molinari, the reigning British Open champion, the world\xe2\x80\x99s seventh-ranked golfer and a winner just last month on the PGA Tour, does not seem perturbed by the disregard.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s obviously loads of great players in golf right now; I think I\xe2\x80\x99m getting the attention that I deserve,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molinari, who played Friday with Tyrrell Hatton and Rafa Cabrera-Bello. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I\xe2\x80\x99m happy to go about my business and keep playing good golf.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Molinari\xe2\x80\x99s climb into golf\xe2\x80\x99s upper echelon, however, may soon be impossible to overlook, even on American soil.'
b'Day also shot 67 as he moved into a tie for the lead. Koepka cooled off slightly after Thursday\xe2\x80\x99s round of 66, shooting 71. Scott looked as if he would have the halfway lead all to himself until his putter let him down and he bogeyed the 16th hole. Oosthuizen, who often plays well at the Masters, charged with a 33 on the back nine.'
b'Dustin Johnson, the world\xe2\x80\x99s second-ranked golfer and another major champion, finished a stroke behind the leaders along with Justin Harding, a Masters rookie from South Africa.'
b'In the end, it was Molinari who played the most consistently, making five birdies and 13 pars.'
b'Molinari first participated in the Masters in 2006 as the caddie for his brother, Edoardo, who qualified as the United States Amateur champion. This is his eighth go-round as a player, and it has been an arduous process to make himself comfortable at Augusta National. Molinari is 24 over par in the 26 rounds he has played, and he has shot 75 or higher eight times, including an 81 in 2013.'
b'Even caddying for his brother was \xe2\x80\x9ca bit of a nightmare,\xe2\x80\x9d Molinari said Friday.'

b'Slowly, Molinari has acclimated. It has not hurt that he has played the best golf of his career in the last 14 months, including a star turn at the Ryder Cup in September. Over his last four rounds at Augusta National, Molinari is 10 under par.'
b'Most of the improvement has been in his putting on the demanding Augusta National greens, which require a precise understanding of subtle contours and topography.'

b'PEBBLE BEACH, Calif. \xe2\x80\x94 The celebration was muted, even if Pebble Beach Golf Links was at its most radiant on Monday morning, with clear skies and bright sunshine casting the Pacific Ocean, the 18th hole and Phil Mickelson in an exquisite light. b'\xe2\x80\x9cPaul made the tough call, but it was the right decision,\xe2\x80\x9d Mickelson said, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes I just get in my own little bubble and I don\xe2\x80\x99t see the big picture.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s decision-making in the caldron of competition can sometimes be a liability, as was evident in his collapse on the final hole of the 2006 U.S. Open at Winged Foot \xe2\x80\x94 or more recently, in his decision to one-time a moving putt during the third round of last year\xe2\x80\x99s U.S. Open at Shinnecock Hills.'
b'But in closing out his 44th PGA Tour victory this week, Mickelson, 48, was a study in restraint. b'Any victory is special at Pebble Beach, a historic venue with personal resonance for Mickelson and his family, but he hopes a more meaningful celebration on the 18th green awaits him in June, for the 119th U.S. Open \xe2\x80\x94 the only major tournament that Mickelson has not won.'
b'Can he strike gold twice at Pebble Beach in the same year? Two men have proved it\xe2\x80\x99s possible; Jack Nicklaus in 1972, on his way to a seven-victory season, and Tiger Woods in 2000, when he won nine PGA Tour events. Nicklaus was in his 30s in 1972 and Woods in his 20s in 2000, both at the peak of their powers.'
b'Mickelson will turn 49 on the final day of this year\xe2\x80\x99s U.S. Open, and a win would make him the oldest men\xe2\x80\x99s major champion, surpassing Julius Boros, who was 48 when he won the 1968 P.G.A. b'Mickelson\xe2\x80\x99s game appears to be aging as well as did the bottle of 1990 Roman\xc3\xa9e-Conti wine that he drank from the Claret Jug after winning the 2013 British Open. He has a second and a first in his last three starts \xe2\x80\x94 with a missed cut at the Phoenix Open sandwiched in between. The resurgence of Mickelson and the 43-year-old Woods has made the men\xe2\x80\x99s game infinitely more compelling.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that if I play my best, if Tiger plays his best, it\xe2\x80\x99s good enough to win on any week,\xe2\x80\x9d Mickelson said, adding, \xe2\x80\x9cI just think that both myself and Tiger are going to have a really, really good year.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mickelson and Woods both have found their way to the winner\xe2\x80\x99s circle more recently than either Jordan Spieth or Rory McIlroy have. Spieth\xe2\x80\x99s turn will come next, at the P.G.A. Championship in May.'
b'And then the spotlight will settle like a laser beam on Mickelson, who played his first major as a pro at the 1992 U.S. Open at Pebble Beach. He noted that this year marked the 100th anniversary of his maternal grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s debut as a pre-teen caddie at Pebble Beach, where he made 25 cents a round.'
b'During the greenside trophy presentation, Mickelson told the story of how his grandfather, Al Santos, who grew up in Monterey, Calif., dropped out of school in the fourth grade to help support his family and shortly thereafter found his way to the Pebble Beach caddie shack.'

b'Playing Pebble Beach has taught Mickelson to take nothing for granted. b'Supported by'
b'By Richard Goldstein'
b'Marilynn Smith, who help found the Ladies Professional Golf Association in 1950, when the women\xe2\x80\x99s game was barely a blip on the national sports scene, and went on to win 21 tour events, including two major championships, died on Tuesday in Goodyear, Ariz. She was 89.'
b'Her death, which was announced by the L.P.G.A. on its website, left Marlene Bauer Hagge and Shirley Spork as the last survivors among the 13 pioneers of the women\xe2\x80\x99s tour.'
b'The L.P.G.A. said Smith, who would have turned 90 on Saturday, made her last public appearance on March 24 greeting players as they walked off the 18th green at the Bank of Hope Founders Cup tournament in Phoenix.'
b'During its first season, the L.P.G.A. sponsored a dozen or so tournaments, with prize money totaling just $50,000. Babe Didrikson Zaharias, an Olympic track and field champion in 1932, and Patty Berg were the main draws at first, but Smith, as well as co-founders like Hagge, Louise Suggs and Betty Jameson, became prominent golf pros in their own right.'
b'Last year, the L.P.G.A. sponsored 32 events in 13 countries with $65.35 million in prize money.'
b'Smith captured the 1963 and 1964 Titleholders Championship, an L.P.G.A. major tournament of her era, which was played at Augusta Country Club in Georgia, adjacent to Augusta National Golf Club, the home of the Masters. She won her first L.P.G.A. tournament in 1954 and her last in 1972.'
b'While serving as L.P.G.A. president from 1958 to 1960, Smith was instrumental in founding the organization\xe2\x80\x99s teaching division, now known as the L.P.G.A. Teaching and Club Professional Membership. She gave clinics throughout the United States and in 37 other countries and was the first woman to be a television analyst for men\xe2\x80\x99s pro golf tournaments. She worked with ABC at the 1973 United States Open, at Oakmont in Pennsylvania, and the Colonial, in Fort Worth.'
b'She was inducted into the World Golf Hall of Fame in 2006.'
b'The L.P.G.A. was the successor to the Women\xe2\x80\x99s Professional Golf Association, which was founded during World War II but disbanded in 1949 after incurring financial difficulties. In the L.P.G.A.\xe2\x80\x99s early years, it struggled as well, receiving little notice in the press or on the radio and having no TV exposure.'
b'The women also lacked the funds to fly, so they traveled to tournaments in caravans of four or five automobiles when the interstate highway system was in its infancy.'
b'When they reached a tournament site, they had to sell their product to the locals. Smith, an exuberant woman who seemed just right for publicizing the tour, was given the nickname Miss Personality for her cheerleading.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe would go to major league ball parks, St. Louis, Cincinnati, Washington, D.C., and hit golf balls from home plate out to center field, with a 9 or an 8 iron, and then we\xe2\x80\x99d get on the microphone and ask those baseball fans to come out and see the L.P.G.A. play,\xe2\x80\x9d she told The Arizona Republic long afterward.'
b'The women were also the tournament directors and rules makers, and they called in results to the news agencies and newspapers. Tournament sites came and went. \xe2\x80\x9cWe might get one event one year; the next year it was gone,\xe2\x80\x9d Smith told The Dallas Morning News in 2000.'
b'Marilynn Smith was born on April 29, 1929, in Topeka, Kan., to Lynn and Alma Smith. Her father was an insurance executive.'
b'In her pre-teenage years, Marilynn managed and pitched for a boys\xe2\x80\x99 baseball team. As she once told golf.com: \xe2\x80\x9cI came home from pitching one day and my mother said, \xe2\x80\x98Well, how did you do today, dear?\xe2\x80\x99 I threw my mitt against the wall and I said, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, \xe2\x80\x94,\xe2\x80\x99 a four-letter word beginning with S that I had learned from the boys on the sandlot. And she marched me into the lavatory and washed my mouth out with soap.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'When her father came home and learned of her outburst, she recalled, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWe better take her out to the Wichita Country Club and teach her a more ladylike sport.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Her childhood dream was to play baseball for the St. Louis Cardinals, but she agreed to take golf lessons from a country-club pro. Captivated by the sport, she went on to win three state amateur championships and the 1949 national collegiate golf championship as a sophomore at the University of Kansas. She turned pro after that when the Spalding sporting goods company gave her a contract to promote its equipment at clinics.'
b'Smith received the first Patty Berg Award for distinguished service to women\xe2\x80\x99s golf in 1979. In her later years she sponsored a tournament raising scholarship money for female high school seniors planning to continue to play golf in college. Living in Goodyear, a suburb of Phoenix, she played recreationally until she was about 70, when her knees gave out. There was no immediate word on her survivors.'
b'Smith estimated that she had given 4,000 clinics in the United States and abroad. On one of her four trips to New Zealand, she inspired a 10-year-old girl named Marilyn Smith to take up golf. In the 1970s, that Marilyn Smith became the first pro player from her country to join the L.P.G.A. Tour.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWith Marilynn Smith as a founding member still playing, they called me MJ Smith,\xe2\x80\x9d she once told Wellington Golf, which advises golf clubs in the vicinity of New Zealand\xe2\x80\x99s capital. \xe2\x80\x9cThough they did send one of my prize checks to her by mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'An earlier version of this obituary misstated the location in Georgia of the Titleholders Championship, which Smith won in 1963 and 1964. It was Augusta Country Club, not Augusta National Golf Club.'

b'By The New York Times'
b'Welcome to Crossing the Border, a limited-run weekly newsletter from The New York Times. Like what you see? Send this to a friend. If someone forwarded it to you, sign up here to have the next issue delivered to your inbox.'
b'By Manny Fernandez in Donna, Tex.'
b'It\xe2\x80\x99s springtime on the border, and that means the winter season is winding down \xe2\x80\x94 the months when tens of thousands of retirees from Minnesota, South Dakota, Illinois and other parts of the Midwest adopt South Texas as their home away from home.'
b'Retirees in swimsuits ride their bicycles to the pool, bright noodles tucked under their arms like jousting lances. They line the streets for golf-cart parades. They venture into Mexico, shopping for cheap medicine, getting pedicures, undergoing low-cost dental work and sipping goblet-sized margaritas.'
b'They are known as Winter Texans, and they concentrate mainly in the Rio Grande Valley, the temperate region deep in South Texas that is also the place where the largest number of migrants has been crossing lately from Central America \xe2\x80\x94 President Trump has declared a national emergency along the border and stationed Army troops to help control the escalating influx. One of their base camps was a quarter-mile from where seniors at the Victoria Palms resort in Donna deploy by the dozens to occupy five pickleball courts.'
b'The annual invasion of Midwesterners \xe2\x80\x94 and a number of Canadians \xe2\x80\x94 has decreased in recent years, but they remain an economic and cultural force. An estimated 106,000 Winter Texans spent about $528 million in the valley in the 2017-18 winter season, according to a survey by the University of Texas Rio Grande Valley. Tex-Mex restaurants fly welcome banners for them. There are Winter Texan newspapers, age-qualified RV parks, expos, cruises, theater productions and bumper stickers \xe2\x80\x94 one pickup truck in the border city of McAllen had two: \xe2\x80\x9cIowa Winter Texan\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m retired. Go around.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt gets me out of the snowbanks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dean Miller, 58, a resident of Detroit Lakes, Minn., who spends the winter living and working at the Winter Ranch resort in Alamo. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll go to some event down here and you\xe2\x80\x99ll find your neighbor from back home.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The majority of Winter Texans are white and in their early 70s. Many of them voted for Mr. Trump in 2016, and their very presence this winter quietly subverted the president\xe2\x80\x99s assertion that the border is in crisis. They have been coming to the region for years \xe2\x80\x94 in some cases, their parents were Winter Texans, too \xe2\x80\x94 and the political dynamics and national-emergency rhetoric has no effect on how, or where, they spend their retirement.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t see what you see on TV,\xe2\x80\x9d said Terry Goss, the general manager of Victoria Palms, one of the largest Winter Texan communities, with up to 2,500 retirees.'
b'What do Winter Texans see, exactly?'
b'A slice of border life that looks nothing like what many Americans think of as border life. There was Nathan\xe2\x80\x99s fourth annual Sock Hop at Ranchero Village in Weslaco one Friday. There were fish frys and riverboat tours, line dancing and karaoke sessions, bluegrass acoustic jams and nondenominational church services. At the Winter Ranch resort in Alamo one Monday afternoon, the Winter Ranch Players packed the house with three one-act plays, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Ethel and Albert Comedies.\xe2\x80\x9d At Victoria Palms, Mr. Goss spoke standing next to a poster listing all the sold-out shows at the resort\xe2\x80\x99s ballroom, one of them a Rolling Stones tribute band concert. Victoria Palms is so popular with Canadians that a Victoria Palms reunion is scheduled most summers near Toronto.'
b'Margaret Hitzemann, 63, who normally lives in Onamia, Minn., spent the past two months in a townhouse in Port Aransas, near Corpus Christi. One rainy afternoon, she and her husband visited the Mexican tourist town of Nuevo Progreso. They bought crispy chapulines \xe2\x80\x94 crickets, a fried delicacy \xe2\x80\x94 and went into a pharmacy to buy some cheap medicine.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t take a lot of meds, so we were thinking, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, let\xe2\x80\x99s go down and get some cheap Tylenol and Advil and that kind of thing,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. Hitzemann said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was like, \xe2\x80\x98Oh my gosh, this isn\xe2\x80\x99t any cheaper than Walmart.\xe2\x80\x99 So we ended up not buying any medications.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In a region with a large Latino population and tolerant attitudes toward immigration \xe2\x80\x94 many people have family members on both sides of the border \xe2\x80\x94 culture clash with the Northerners is inevitable. The study released by the University of Texas Rio Grande Valley included unedited messages from Winter Texans to local officials, and some of the sharpest comments read: \xe2\x80\x9cBe more American, less Mexican. This is not Mexico!\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cHave channel 5 respect Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cKeep the grass cut, the trash picked up and speak English.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Already, the exodus back north has started. The end of the season for many comes on Thursday, with the annual Winter Texan appreciation day in Nuevo Progreso.'
b'For some, the season never ends. There are Converted Texans, former Winter Texans who settle in the valley for good. Kristi Collier, a McAllen native who runs a media and hospitality company that caters to Winter Texans called Welcome Home, Rio Grande Valley, hosts an annual Converted Texan Fiesta in April. (She leads a swearing-in ceremony.)'
b'Among the ranks of the converted are Ken and Lois Lane, originally from Anchorage, who started spending winters at Victoria Palms in 2005. They now live there permanently in a double-wide mobile home.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think the cost of living here is very reasonable, compared to many other places,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Lane, 73, a retired power dispatcher for an electrical company.'
b'He and his wife visited Nuevo Progreso and hosted their son and his family from Iowa over spring break. The couple is in a unique category: sort of reverse Winter Texans.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe motor home we kept and in the summer when it gets real hot, we go up north,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Lane said.'
b'Manny is one of a team of New York Times journalists currently reporting from the border. Each week they\xe2\x80\x99ll be sharing a slice of their reporting about the border and the people who spend time on both sides of it.'
b'Do you have questions about life on the border? Or feedback about this newsletter? Email us at: crossingtheborder@nytimes.com.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThere are people out there who just say, \xe2\x80\x98Send them all back and build a wall.\xe2\x80\x99 But they would be facing empty shelves in the grocery store if that were to happen.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x94 Mike McMahon, a dairy farmer in upstate New York.'
b'The farms of upstate New York are thousands of miles from the southern border, but close enough to the northern one that the Border Patrol has jurisdiction. Farmers\xe2\x80\x99 reliance on undocumented workers had made the region a focal point in the debate over the Trump administration\xe2\x80\x99s border policies, leading to tensions between state politicians of both parties and the federal agents enforcing the president\xe2\x80\x99s crackdown on illegal immigration.'
b'Christina Goldbaum, an immigration reporter in The New York Times\xe2\x80\x99s Metro department, explored Mr. McMahon\xe2\x80\x99s story, and those of migrants who work at farms like his, in an article this week. Read more about it here.'
b'This week, KPBS, a public radio station in Southern California, launched a new podcast, \xe2\x80\x9cOnly Here,\xe2\x80\x9d about life and culture in San Diego and Tijuana \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cthe art, food, traditions and culture that exists because of the border,\xe2\x80\x9d they write. The first episode profiles a young photographer who is gay and undocumented, and you can listen to it, along with other episodes, here.'
b'Podcast creators, like everyone else, have been drawn to stories and conversations about the border. If you prefer your stories in audio form, here are a few more to get you started:'
b'\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\x9cThe Green Line,\xe2\x80\x9d produced by the union of Border Patrol agents. The hosts, Art Del Cueto and Brandon Judd, are Border Patrol agents who have been supportive of President Trump\xe2\x80\x99s policies \xe2\x80\x94 and both appeared at his side in the White House briefing room in January when he talked about declaring a national emergency to help build a border wall. (The Times has written about them before.)'
b'\xe2\x80\xa2 Last spring, WNYC\xe2\x80\x99s Radiolab ran \xe2\x80\x9cBorder Project,\xe2\x80\x9d a three-part series about a Border Patrol policy that most likely led to a rise, beginning in the late 1990s, in the number of migrants who died while crossing to the United States. It also profiles some of the migrants who made the trek despite it. Listen to all the episodes here.'
b'\xe2\x80\xa2 This episode of PRX\xe2\x80\x99s Radio Diaries, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Border Wall,\xe2\x80\x9d focuses on those strange parts of the border that one woman calls a \xe2\x80\x9cno-man\xe2\x80\x99s land,\xe2\x80\x9d cut off from their country by a barrier that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t always follow the line it\xe2\x80\x99s meant to.'
b'\xe2\x80\xa2 Our very own \xe2\x80\x9cDaily\xe2\x80\x9d podcast has many great episodes from the border. Start with a two-part series, \xe2\x80\x9cDispatches From the Border\xe2\x80\x9d (Part 1 here and Part 2 here), which follows Azam Ahmed, the Times bureau chief for Mexico, Central America and the Caribbean, and other Times journalists on a trip along the nearly 2,000-mile border with Mexico. Then there\xe2\x80\x99s this episode, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Scars of Family Separation,\xe2\x80\x9d about a migrant father who crossed the border with his daughter. Finally, from last summer, \xe2\x80\x9cHow Separating Migrant Families Became U.S. Policy\xe2\x80\x9d is an interview with Stephen Miller, a senior policy adviser who has helped the president frame his immigration policy.'
